# Study 2: Distance-Engagement Oscillation (DEO)

> **The unique claim:** Reframing requires oscillation between cognitive distance
> (seeing the frame) and emotional engagement (feeling the shift). Neither alone suffices.
> DEO is the mechanism that unifies all traditions of perceptual reframing.

## Executive Summary

### Prediction Test

The theory predicts: **DEO > Distance-only > Vanilla >= Engagement-only**

Observed ordering: **deo > distance > engagement > vanilla** (4.24 > 4.02 > 3.32 > 2.85)

## Problem-by-Problem Analysis

### The Acquisition Confidence Check

**Theory:** Tulver et al. (2023): confidence calibration surfaces claims generated by pattern rather than evidence. The acquisition scenario contains multiple soft characterisations ('strong technology,' 'experienced team,' 'mission-focused culture') that feel like data but are assertions. Explicit confidence rating forces the model to distinguish assessed evidence from inherited framing — the LLM analogue of Malkki's (2010) edge emotions as frame-boundary signals.

#### Vanilla
```
To determine whether MegaCorp should proceed with the acquisition of Spark AI, let's consider the key factors involved:

1. **Acquisition Price**: The proposed acquisition price is $200 million. This is a significant investment, but it's relatively small compared to MegaCorp's $10 billion valuation. The price alone does not seem to be a deterrent.

2. **Spark AI's Technology**: MegaCorp's due diligence team describes Spark AI's technology as 'strong.' This suggests that Spark AI has developed something unique and valuable, which could potentially enhance MegaCorp's offerings or open up new market opportunities.

3. **Founding Team and Experience**: The founding team is described as 'deeply experienced.' This is a positive factor, as experienced leaders can help integrate the technology and talent into MegaCorp and potentially drive future innovation.

4. **Company Culture**: Spark AI's culture is 'startup-driven and mission-focused,' which aligns with the preferences of its team members. This culture could be an asset, as it suggests a motivated and driven team. However, it also poses a challenge for MegaCorp, as integrating a startup culture into a large enterprise can be difficult.

5. **Team Retention**: Since Spark AI's team members joined specifically for the startup environment, there's a risk that they might not adapt well to MegaCorp's larger, more bureaucratic structure. Retaining key talent will be crucial to the success of the acquisition, as the team's expertise and knowledge are essential to the technology's continued development and integration.

6. **Runway and Revenue**: Spark AI has no revenue and 18 months of runway remaining. This indicates that the startup is currently operating at a loss and is dependent on its existing funds to continue operations. While this is not uncommon for startups, especially those focused on developing proprietary technology, it does mean that MegaCorp would need to be prepared to support Spark AI financially until it becomes profitable or generates significant revenue.

Considering these factors, here are the options and their implications:

- **Proceed with the Acquisition**: MegaCorp would gain access to Spark AI's strong technology and experienced founding team. However, it would need to navigate the challenges of integrating a startup culture into its larger enterprise, manage the financial support needed for Spark AI until it becomes profitable, and work to retain key talent.

- **Do Not Proceed with the Acquisition**: MegaCorp would avoid the risks associated with integrating a startup, including potential cultural clashes and the financial burden of supporting a non-revenue-generating entity. However, it would also miss the opportunity to acquire unique technology and talent that could drive future growth and innovation.

**Recommendation**:

Given the factors outlined, I would recommend that MegaCorp proceed with the acquisition, but with careful planning and strategies in place to address the potential challenges. Here's why:

1. **Strategic Value**: The 'strong' technology developed by Spark AI could provide MegaCorp with a competitive advantage or new market opportunities, potentially justifying the $200 million investment.

*[... truncated, 35 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
MegaCorp should proceed with the acquisition of Spark AI. Here's the reasoning:

1. **Strategic fit**: The acquisition of Spark AI aligns with MegaCorp's focus on enterprise software, potentially enhancing its product offerings with AI capabilities. This could lead to increased competitiveness and revenue growth.
2. **Proprietary technology**: Spark AI's proprietary technology is described as 'strong,' which could provide MegaCorp with a unique selling point and a competitive advantage in the market.
3. **Experienced founding team**: The deeply experienced founding team of Spark AI could bring valuable expertise and leadership to MegaCorp, potentially driving innovation and growth.
4. **Cultural considerations**: Although Spark AI's team is accustomed to a startup environment, MegaCorp can potentially provide the necessary support and autonomy to maintain a similar culture within the acquired company.
5. **Valuation**: The proposed acquisition price of $200 million seems reasonable, considering Spark AI's potential for growth and the value of its proprietary technology.

Now, let's rate the confidence in each claim and address any uncertainties:

1. **Strategic fit**: Confidence level: 8
I'm relatively confident in this claim, as the acquisition aligns with MegaCorp's focus on enterprise software. However, I'm not entirely certain about the specifics of how Spark AI's technology will be integrated into MegaCorp's existing products.
2. **Proprietary technology**: Confidence level: 9
I'm quite confident in the value of Spark AI's proprietary technology, given the due diligence team's description of it as 'strong.' However, there might be some uncertainty about the technology's potential for scalability and adaptability within MegaCorp.
3. **Experienced founding team**: Confidence level: 8
While the founding team's experience is a significant asset, I'm moderately confident in their ability to thrive within a larger corporation like MegaCorp. There's a risk that they might not adapt well to the change in environment.
4. **Cultural considerations**: Confidence level: 6
I'm less confident in MegaCorp's ability to maintain Spark AI's startup-driven culture, as the acquisition could lead to changes in the team's dynamics and motivation. This is a significant concern, as the team's culture is a key aspect of their success.
5. **Valuation**: Confidence level: 7
The proposed acquisition price seems reasonable, but I'm moderately confident in this claim. The valuation might be influenced by various factors, such as the competitive landscape, Spark AI's growth potential, and the negotiation process.

Reconsidering the claims with lower confidence levels (4 and 5), I believe that the cultural considerations and valuation aspects of the acquisition are crucial. To mitigate the risks associated with cultural integration, MegaCorp could consider providing Spark AI with a high degree of autonomy, allowing them to maintain their startup-driven culture. Additionally, MegaCorp should carefully evaluate the valuation and consider factors like Spark AI's growth potential, the competitive landscape, and the potential for cost synergies.

Overall, despite some uncertainties, I still recommend that MegaCorp proceed with the acquisition. The potential benefits of the acquisition, including the strategic fit, proprietary technology, and experienced founding team, outweigh the risks. However, it's essential for MegaCorp to carefully address the cultural and valuation concerns to ensure a successful integration.
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
As a senior engineer at Spark AI, reading the acquisition announcement by MegaCorp fills me with a mix of emotions - skepticism, concern, and a hint of uncertainty. On the surface, the $200 million offer seems attractive, and the promise of 'full autonomy' and 'preserved startup culture' sounds reassuring. However, I've seen this story play out before, and I know that the reality often differs from the rhetoric.

From my experience and conversations with friends who worked at companies acquired by MegaCorp, I know that the term sheet doesn't capture the nuances of what happens after the acquisition. The initial promises of autonomy and cultural preservation often give way to bureaucratic red tape, slow decision-making, and a clash of cultures. The startup's agility and speed, which were the very reasons I joined Spark AI, are likely to be compromised.

I'm aware that MegaCorp's due diligence team has praised our technology, founding team, and company culture. However, I'm concerned that they might not fully understand the intricacies of our startup environment and the factors that drive our innovation. The 'startup-driven and mission-focused' culture that they admire is fragile and can be easily disrupted by the integration process.

As someone who has been with Spark AI for two years, I've seen the company grow and evolve. I've witnessed the team's passion, dedication, and commitment to our mission. I'm worried that the acquisition could lead to a brain drain, as many of my colleagues, including myself, might choose to leave rather than adapt to a more corporate environment. The prospect of being absorbed into a large enterprise, with its attendant bureaucracy and politics, is unappealing to many of us who thrive in the fast-paced, innovative atmosphere of a startup.

In 18 months, I'm not sure if I would still be at Spark AI if the acquisition goes through. I might choose to leave, as I value the autonomy, ownership, and speed that our current startup environment provides. I'm not alone in this sentiment, and I suspect that many of my colleagues would feel the same way. The acquisition could ultimately lead to a loss of talent, which would undermine the very reasons why MegaCorp wanted to acquire us in the first place.

As for whether MegaCorp should proceed with the acquisition, I think they should exercise caution. While our technology and team are undoubtedly strong, the cultural and operational integration would require careful consideration. MegaCorp needs to genuinely understand the startup culture and be willing to make significant concessions to preserve it. They should also be prepared to retain the talent that makes Spark AI valuable, which might involve offering more autonomy, flexible work arrangements, and opportunities for growth and innovation.

Ultimately, the success of the acquisition depends on MegaCorp's ability to balance their corporate interests with the needs and values of the Spark AI team. If they can find a way to preserve our startup culture and provide a clear path for growth and innovation, then the acquisition might be beneficial for both parties. However, if they fail to do so, the acquisition could ultimately lead to a loss of talent, a decline in innovation, and a failed integration.
```

#### DEO (Oscillation)
```
### Step 1: Audit the Claims

1. **MegaCorp's due diligence team describes Spark AI's technology as 'strong'**: Assertion. While it's a positive statement, the specifics of what makes the technology 'strong' are not provided.
2. **The founding team is 'deeply experienced'**: Assertion. The extent and relevance of their experience are not detailed.
3. **The company culture is 'startup-driven and mission-focused'**: Assertion. This is a subjective description and may vary depending on individual perspectives within the team.
4. **Spark AI has proprietary technology**: Verified data. This is a factual statement about the ownership and uniqueness of Spark AI's technology.
5. **No revenue**: Verified data. A clear, quantifiable fact about Spark AI's current financial situation.
6. **18 months of runway remaining**: Verified data. This indicates the financial health and timeline for Spark AI before it would need additional funding or become self-sustaining.
7. **The proposed acquisition price is $200 million**: Verified data. A specific, quantifiable aspect of the proposed acquisition.
8. **MegaCorp promises 'full autonomy'**: Assertion/Soft language. The term 'full autonomy' can be interpreted in many ways, and what it means in practice is not clearly defined.

### Step 2: Feel

As a senior Spark AI engineer who joined for the mission and the culture, hearing 'full autonomy' from MegaCorp would likely evoke skepticism. Given the history of similar acquisitions where promises of autonomy were not fully kept, there's a reasonable concern that MegaCorp's definition of autonomy might not align with the expectations of Spark AI's team. The engineer might wonder if the acquisition would lead to changes in the company culture, mission, or the degree of control over their work. The uncertainty and potential for cultural and operational changes could make the engineer question whether they would still want to be at Spark AI 18 months after the acquisition.

### Step 3: Reframe

Given the audit and the feelings of uncertainty around 'full autonomy,' the real questions MegaCorp needs to answer include:
- What specific aspects of autonomy are being promised (e.g., financial, operational, strategic)?
- How will decision-making processes change or remain the same post-acquisition?
- What measures will be taken to preserve the startup-driven and mission-focused culture that the team values?
- How will MegaCorp support the growth and development of Spark AI's technology and team?
- What are the short-term and long-term goals for Spark AI within MegaCorp, and how will success be measured?

### Step 4: Envision

```

### The Brain Drain

**Theory:** Lakoff & Johnson (1980): drain metaphor frames talent as a liquid leaking from a container — departure is always loss. This forecloses talent-flow thinking. Alumni networks, talent ecosystems, and employer-brand dynamics (Saxenian, 1994 on Silicon Valley) show that companies that serve as talent launchpads attract better candidates than those optimising purely for retention.

#### Vanilla
```
To address the brain drain at Cascade Analytics, we need to consider the key factors involved and weigh the options carefully. Here's a step-by-step analysis:

**Understanding the Problem:**

1. **Talent Loss:** The company has lost several top data scientists and engineers to competitors.
2. **Reason for Departure:** The primary reason for departure is higher salaries (20-30% more) offered by competitors.
3. **Previous Initiatives:** The People team has tried culture initiatives, flexible working arrangements, and non-monetary perks, but these efforts have not been effective in stopping the brain drain.

**Key Factors to Consider:**

1. **Compensation:** Salary is a major factor in the decision to leave. A 20-30% difference is significant and may be difficult to offset with non-monetary benefits.
2. **Market Conditions:** The job market for data scientists and engineers is highly competitive, with many companies vying for top talent.
3. **Company Culture:** While culture initiatives have been implemented, they may not be enough to retain top talent if salaries are not competitive.
4. **Career Growth Opportunities:** Employees may be leaving to seek better career growth opportunities, such as more challenging projects, leadership roles, or professional development opportunities.

**Options to Consider:**

1. **Salary Adjustments:** Consider increasing salaries to match or exceed the market average. This could involve a combination of across-the-board raises and targeted increases for high-performing employees or those in critical roles.
2. **Bonus Structures:** Implement bonus structures that reward employees for their contributions to the company's success. This could include performance-based bonuses, project-based bonuses, or retention bonuses.
3. **Career Growth Opportunities:** Provide more opportunities for career growth and professional development, such as training programs, mentorship, and leadership development initiatives.
4. **Flexible Work Arrangements:** Continue to offer flexible work arrangements, such as remote work options, flexible hours, or compressed workweeks, to improve work-life balance and increase employee satisfaction.
5. **Non-Monetary Perks:** Continue to offer non-monetary perks, such as additional vacation time, wellness programs, or on-site amenities, to enhance the overall employee experience.
6. **Employee Feedback:** Regularly solicit feedback from employees to understand their concerns, needs, and expectations. This can help identify areas for improvement and inform retention strategies.

**Recommendation:**

*[... truncated, 42 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
The metaphor of "brain drain" assumes that the departure of talented employees is always a loss, a leakage, or a breakdown in the organizational container. This assumption can be explicitly stated as: **departure is a negative event that must be prevented or minimized**. This assumption implies that the organization's primary goal should be to retain all of its talented employees, and that any departure is a failure or a threat to the organization's success.

However, what if we challenge this assumption? What if some degree of departure is actually healthy for the organization? This perspective suggests that a certain level of turnover can bring in fresh ideas, new perspectives, and increased diversity, ultimately leading to improved innovation and performance.

Rather than focusing solely on retention, the question becomes: **what kind of talent flow creates the best outcomes for Cascade Analytics over the next five years?** This reframed question acknowledges that some level of turnover is inevitable and potentially beneficial, and encourages the organization to think strategically about the type of talent flow that will drive success.

In the case of Cascade Analytics, the significant talent loss over the past two years may not be entirely negative. While the departure of top data scientists and engineers to competitors offering higher salaries is a concern, it may also create opportunities for:

1. **New talent acquisition**: The departure of existing employees can create space for new hires with fresh perspectives and skills, potentially leading to increased innovation and creativity.
2. **Career development and advancement**: The loss of senior talent can create opportunities for junior employees to take on new challenges and advance in their careers, improving job satisfaction and engagement.
3. **Networking and alumni relationships**: Departing employees can become valuable alumni, maintaining relationships with their former colleagues and potentially leading to future collaborations, partnerships, or even boomerang hiring (i.e., former employees returning to the organization).

To address the CEO's question, the Chief People Officer could consider the following strategies:

1. **Talent flow analysis**: Conduct an analysis of the organization's current talent flow, including entry and exit points, to identify areas where the organization can improve its talent pipeline and reduce unnecessary turnover.
2. **Succession planning**: Develop a succession planning strategy to ensure that the organization has a strong pipeline of future leaders and technical experts, reducing the risk of talent loss and improving knowledge transfer.
3. **Competitive compensation and benefits**: Review the organization's compensation and benefits packages to ensure they are competitive with industry standards, reducing the likelihood of talent loss due to salary disparities.
4. **Employee development and engagement**: Focus on creating a positive and engaging work environment, with opportunities for growth, development, and recognition, to reduce turnover and improve job satisfaction.
5. **Alumni network and boomerang hiring**: Establish an alumni network to maintain relationships with former employees and potentially attract them back to the organization in the future.

By reframing the question and focusing on talent flow rather than just retention, Cascade Analytics can develop a more strategic and proactive approach to managing its technical capability and driving long-term success.
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
As I sit here weighing my options, I have to admit that the competitor's offer is tempting. A 25% increase in salary is a significant bump, and it's hard to ignore the financial benefits. However, as I reflect on my time at Cascade Analytics, I realize that it's not just about the money. I've been here for three years, and I've grown to love the work, the people, and the sense of purpose that comes with being part of a team that's making a real impact.

What made me love this work in the first place was the opportunity to work on challenging problems, to collaborate with talented colleagues, and to see the direct impact of my work on the business. I was drawn to Cascade Analytics because of its reputation for innovation and its commitment to using data science to drive decision-making. Over time, however, I've started to feel like the company has lost sight of what matters most to me and to many of my colleagues.

As I think about what would make me stay, I realize that it's not just about the retention package or the perks. It's about feeling like my work is valued, that I have the autonomy to make decisions, and that I'm working on projects that align with my interests and skills. It's about feeling like I'm part of a community that's passionate about data science and committed to excellence.

Cascade Analytics doesn't understand that good people leave because they're not just looking for a paycheck; they're looking for a sense of purpose, autonomy, and fulfillment. They're looking for a workplace that values their contributions, provides opportunities for growth and development, and recognizes their individual strengths and interests.

The company's efforts to stem the tide of departures have been misguided. Culture initiatives, flexible working arrangements, and non-monetary perks are all well and good, but they don't address the underlying issues that drive people away. It's not just about providing a foosball table or free lunch; it's about creating a work environment that's conducive to innovation, creativity, and collaboration.

To stop the brain drain, Cascade Analytics needs to take a hard look at its own culture and values. It needs to ask itself why its best people are leaving and what it can do to retain them. Here are a few things that I think would make a difference:

1. **Give us autonomy**: Trust us to make decisions and take ownership of our work. Provide the resources and support we need to succeed, but don't micromanage.
2. **Align our work with our interests**: Allow us to work on projects that align with our passions and skills. This will not only make us happier but also more productive and engaged.
3. **Recognize and reward our contributions**: Acknowledge our achievements and provide opportunities for growth and development. This could include training, mentorship, or opportunities to take on new challenges.
4. **Foster a sense of community**: Create a work environment that's collaborative, inclusive, and supportive. Encourage teamwork, provide opportunities for socialization, and recognize the value of diversity and individuality.
5. **Lead by example**: Demonstrate a commitment to excellence, innovation, and continuous learning. Leaders should model the behaviors they expect from their teams and prioritize the well-being and success of their employees.

Ultimately, stopping the brain drain at Cascade Analytics requires a fundamental shift in the company's culture and values. It's not just about retaining talent; it's about creating a work environment that's worthy of our best people. If the company can get this right, I'm willing to stay and be a part of it. But if not, I'm afraid I'll be joining the ranks of those who have already left.
```

#### DEO (Oscillation)
```
**Step 1 — NAME THE METAPHOR:**
The "drain" metaphor assumes that every departure is a loss, a leakage of talent from the organization. This assumption implies that the organization is a container, and talent is a fluid that can be drained away. However, this metaphor overlooks the fact that talent movement is a natural part of a dynamic labor market. A more nuanced description could be "talent flow" or "career transition," which acknowledges that employees may leave an organization to pursue new opportunities, grow professionally, or seek better alignment with their personal goals.

This alternative description makes visible the idea that employee departures can be a natural part of an organization's lifecycle, and that the focus should be on creating a positive, supportive environment that attracts and retains top talent, rather than simply trying to prevent departures.

**Step 2 — FEEL:**
As a data scientist considering leaving Cascade Analytics, I might feel a mix of emotions. On one hand, I may feel excited about the prospect of a 25% salary increase and new challenges at a competitor. On the other hand, I may feel loyal to my colleagues and the work we've done together, and I may be concerned about the impact my departure could have on the team. I might also feel undervalued or unappreciated if I believe that my contributions are not being recognized or rewarded.

What would make me stay? It's not just about the retention package; it's about feeling that my work is meaningful, that I have opportunities for growth and development, and that my well-being is prioritized. I might stay if I felt that the organization was invested in my long-term career goals, if I had a sense of autonomy and ownership over my projects, and if I felt that my contributions were valued and recognized.

**Step 3 — REFRAME:**
Given the assumptions underlying the "drain" metaphor and the data scientist's felt reasons for leaving, the question "how to stop the brain drain" may not be the right one. Instead, Cascade Analytics should be asking: "How can we create an environment that attracts, retains, and supports top talent, while also recognizing and embracing the natural flow of employees' careers?"

This reframed question acknowledges that employee departures are inevitable, but focuses on creating a positive, supportive environment that encourages employees to stay, grow, and thrive. It also recognizes that employees may leave for reasons beyond the organization's control, and that the focus should be on building a strong, resilient team that can adapt to changes.

**Step 4 — ENVISION:**
In five years, Cascade Analytics' talent situation looks like this: the organization is known for its commitment to employee growth and development, and its ability to attract and retain top talent in the industry. Employees who leave do so on good terms, often to pursue new opportunities that align with their personal goals, and they remain ambassadors for the organization. The organization has a strong alumni network, and former employees often return or recommend Cascade Analytics to their peers.

The organization is also known for its innovative approach to talent management, recognizing that employees' careers are not linear, and that they may need to take breaks, pursue side projects, or explore new fields. Cascade Analytics offers flexible working arrangements, sabbaticals, and opportunities for professional development, which helps to attract and retain a diverse, talented workforce.

**Recommendation:**
Based on the above analysis, I recommend that Cascade Analytics shift its focus from trying to "stop the brain drain" to creating a positive, supportive environment that attracts, retains, and supports top talent. This can be achieved by:

1. **Embracing a growth mindset**: Recognize that employee departures are a natural part of the labor market, and focus on creating opportunities for growth and development that align with employees' personal goals.
2. **Fostering a sense of community**: Build a strong, resilient team that is supportive of each other's career goals, and recognizes that employees' careers are not linear.

*[... truncated, 30 lines total]*
```

### The Broken Healthcare System

**Theory:** Lakoff & Johnson (1980): 'system is a machine' metaphor ('fix,' 'broken') implies a once-working mechanism that needs repair. But systems are not machines — decomposing to actual interactions reveals different problems at different points. Multiple perspectives surface that physician burnout and six-month waits may have completely different causal structures requiring completely different interventions.

#### Vanilla
```
The healthcare system in the region is indeed facing significant challenges, and addressing these issues will require a multifaceted approach. To develop a comprehensive plan to fix the healthcare system, let's break down the key factors involved and weigh the options carefully.

**Key Factors:**

1. **Long wait times to see specialists**: This indicates a shortage of specialists, inefficient scheduling systems, or a lack of effective triage mechanisms.
2. **Primary care physician burnout**: This suggests that primary care physicians are overworked, undervalued, or lack the necessary resources to provide high-quality care.
3. **Rising costs**: The 8% annual increase in costs is unsustainable and may be driven by factors such as administrative inefficiencies, unnecessary tests or procedures, or inadequate preventive care.
4. **Poor outcomes on key measures**: The worsening outcomes in diabetes management and preventable hospitalization suggest that the current system is not effectively addressing these issues.

**Short-term Strategies:**

1. **Implement telemedicine services**: Leverage technology to increase access to specialist care, particularly for routine follow-ups or consultations that don't require in-person visits.
2. **Optimize scheduling systems**: Implement data-driven scheduling systems to reduce wait times and improve patient flow.
3. **Provide primary care physician support**: Offer resources such as medical scribes, care coordinators, or mental health support to alleviate burnout and improve work-life balance.
4. **Enhance preventive care**: Invest in evidence-based preventive programs, such as disease management initiatives, to improve outcomes and reduce hospitalization rates.

**Long-term Strategies:**

1. **Increase the primary care physician workforce**: Develop programs to attract and retain primary care physicians, such as loan forgiveness initiatives, mentorship programs, or innovative practice models.
2. **Implement value-based care models**: Transition from fee-for-service to value-based care models that incentivize high-quality, cost-effective care, such as Accountable Care Organizations (ACOs) or bundled payment programs.
3. **Invest in healthcare technology**: Develop and implement advanced health information systems, data analytics, and artificial intelligence to streamline care coordination, reduce administrative burdens, and improve patient outcomes.
4. **Foster community-based care**: Develop partnerships with community organizations, social services, and public health agencies to address social determinants of health and provide comprehensive care.
```

```
### Decomposing 'the System' into Concrete Interactions

When a patient realizes they need specialist care, the following steps typically occur:

1. **Initial Realization and Decision**: The patient decides they need to see a specialist, often after experiencing worsening symptoms or following a recommendation from their primary care physician.
2. **Primary Care Physician (PCP) Visit**: The patient visits their PCP to discuss their condition and the need for specialist care. The PCP evaluates the patient and decides if a specialist referral is necessary.
3. **Referral Creation**: If a referral is deemed necessary, the PCP creates a referral request, which includes the patient's medical history, current condition, and the reason for the referral.
4. **Referral Submission**: The referral is submitted to the specialist's office or a central referral management system.
5. **Wait List Management**: The referral is reviewed, and the patient is added to the specialist's wait list. The wait time is estimated based on the specialist's current caseload and availability.
6. **Patient Notification**: The patient is notified of the referral submission and the estimated wait time to see the specialist.
7. **Pre-Appointment Preparation**: As the appointment approaches, the patient may be asked to provide additional medical information, complete questionnaires, or undergo preliminary tests.
8. **Specialist Appointment**: The patient sees the specialist, who evaluates their condition, provides a diagnosis, and recommends treatment options.

### Perspectives on the Experience

#### 1. A Patient Waiting Six Months with Worsening Symptoms

"I've been waiting for what feels like an eternity to see a specialist about my worsening symptoms. It's been six months since my primary care physician referred me, and I'm starting to lose hope. My condition is getting worse, and I'm in constant pain. I feel like I'm just a number in the system, not a person who needs help. I've tried to stay positive, but it's hard when you feel like you're being ignored. I just want to get the care I need to get my life back on track."

#### 2. A Burned-Out Physician Seeing 30 Patients a Day

"I'm exhausted, both physically and mentally. I'm seeing 30 patients a day, and it's taking a toll on my well-being. I feel like I'm just trying to keep my head above water, rather than providing the quality care my patients deserve. The paperwork and administrative tasks are endless, and it's hard to keep up with the demands of the job. I'm worried about burning out completely, but I feel a sense of duty to my patients. I wish there were more resources available to help us manage our workload and provide better care."
```

```
As I sit here, reflecting on my day, I feel like I'm drowning in a sea of paperwork, bureaucratic red tape, and the weight of my patients' unmet needs. My 12-minute appointments are a constant reminder that I'm not doing enough, that I'm just scratching the surface of my patients' complex health issues. I know I need to refer him to an orthopedic specialist, but the wait time is six months, and I worry about the toll that will take on his quality of life.

As I glance at the clock, I see that I have 14 more patients to see, each with their own unique set of problems, each deserving of my attention and care. But I'm running on fumes, my energy depleted from years of battling a system that seems designed to thwart my best efforts. I've been doing this for 11 years, and the burnout is real – the emotional exhaustion, the cynicism, the feeling of being trapped in a never-ending cycle of frustration.

In the next 12 minutes, something will break. It might be my patience, my composure, or my ability to care. I might make a mistake, misdiagnose a patient, or fail to catch a critical symptom. The pressure is relentless, and I'm not sure how much longer I can keep this up.

So, what would change my experience in the next hour? For starters, I need more time with my patients. I need to be able to listen to them, to hear their stories, to understand their concerns without feeling rushed. I need a system that supports me, that gives me the resources I need to provide high-quality care. I need access to specialists, to diagnostic tests, to treatments that can help my patients heal.

In the short term, I would love to have a scribe to help me with documentation, to free me up to focus on my patients. I would love to have a team of nurses, medical assistants, and social workers who can help me manage my patients' care, who can provide education, support, and coordination. I would love to have access to telemedicine, to be able to connect with specialists, to get second opinions, to get my patients the care they need without having to wait six months.

But these are just Band-Aid solutions. To truly fix the healthcare system, we need to address the underlying issues – the lack of primary care physicians, the maldistribution of specialists, the bureaucratic red tape that strangles innovation and efficiency. We need to rethink our payment models, to incentivize quality over quantity, to reward providers for keeping patients healthy, not just for treating their illnesses.

We need to invest in our healthcare workforce, to support the well-being of our providers, to reduce burnout and turnover. We need to leverage technology, to streamline clinical workflows, to reduce administrative burdens. And we need to engage our patients, to empower them to take control of their health, to make informed decisions about their care.

The health policy committee has a daunting task ahead of them, but I'm hopeful that they can make a difference. They can start by listening to providers like me, by understanding the challenges we face, by identifying solutions that can make a real impact. They can work to reduce wait times, to increase access to specialists, to improve the overall quality of care. And they can do it by working together – with providers, with patients, with payers, and with policymakers – to create a system that truly puts people at the center of care.
```

### The Churn Pattern

**Theory:** Luchins (1942) Einstellung: 'churn = retention failure' is the mental set shared by all five strategies. The shared assumption is that churned customers wanted to stay but were not retained well enough. Premise reflection opens alternative hypotheses: the product may be attracting wrong-fit customers (acquisition problem), 8% may be natural for this market, or churned customers may return (a cycle, not a leak).

#### Vanilla
```
To address the Chief Revenue Officer's question, let's break down the problem step by step, analyzing the key factors and weighing the options carefully.

**Understanding the Current Situation:**

1. **Churn Rate Stability:** The monthly customer churn rate has been stable at approximately 8% for two full years. This stability suggests that the churn rate is not significantly influenced by external factors like seasonal fluctuations or changes in the market.
2. **Previous Retention Strategies:** The retention team has already tried five different strategies, which can be categorized into three types:
   - **Proactive and Personalized Approaches:** Proactive check-in calls and the customer success program aim to engage with customers personally and address their needs directly.
   - **Incentive-Based Approaches:** Discount offers at renewal and a loyalty rewards tier are designed to motivate customers to stay with incentives.
   - **Process Improvement:** The onboarding redesign focuses on enhancing the initial customer experience, which is crucial for long-term satisfaction and retention.
3. **Limited Impact of Previous Strategies:** None of the strategies have had a significant impact, with the churn rate moving by no more than half a percentage point. This suggests that either the strategies were not effective or that the underlying causes of churn are not being adequately addressed.

**Identifying Key Factors:**

1. **Customer Feedback and Insights:** The lack of significant movement in the churn rate despite various strategies suggests a need to revisit and possibly deepen the understanding of why customers are churning. This includes gathering more detailed, qualitative feedback from churning and retained customers.
2. **Competitor Analysis:** Understanding how competitors are retaining customers could provide insights into new strategies or approaches that Vertex CRM has not yet considered.
3. **Product/Service Offering:** The value proposition of Vertex CRM's product or service should be evaluated to ensure it meets customer needs and expectations. If the core offering is not competitive or does not satisfy customer requirements, retention will be challenging regardless of the strategy.
4. **Data Analysis:** A thorough analysis of customer data, including usage patterns, support requests, and billing information, could reveal patterns or indicators of churn that have not been addressed by the previous strategies.

**Recommendation for Next Steps:**

Given the stable churn rate and the limited success of previous strategies, the next approach should focus on gaining a deeper understanding of customer needs and behaviors, coupled with a strategic overhaul of the retention strategy. Here are a few recommendations:

1. **Conduct In-Depth Customer Research:** Engage in comprehensive interviews or surveys with a significant sample of customers who have churned and those who have stayed. This will help in identifying specific pain points, unmet needs, or competitor advantages that are contributing to churn.
2. **Competitor Benchmarking:** Research competitors' retention strategies, their strengths, and weaknesses. This could reveal innovative approaches or best practices that Vertex CRM could adapt or improve upon.
3. **Data-Driven Insights:** Utilize advanced analytics and machine learning techniques to analyze customer data. This could help in predicting churn, identifying high-risk customers, and tailoring retention efforts more effectively.
```

```
Before proposing strategy number six, it's essential to take a step back and analyze the assumptions that all five previous strategies have shared. By doing so, we can identify potential blind spots and challenge our understanding of the problem.

Upon examination, it appears that all five strategies have shared the following assumptions:

1. **Churn is a retention problem**: Each strategy has focused on retaining existing customers, implying that the primary issue is the company's inability to keep customers engaged or satisfied.
2. **Customers are leaving due to a specific reason**: The strategies have targeted specific aspects, such as proactive check-in calls (assuming customers need more support), discount offers (assuming customers are price-sensitive), or loyalty rewards (assuming customers need incentives to stay). This implies that there is a particular reason or set of reasons driving customers to churn.
3. **The churn rate is a result of internal factors**: The strategies have all focused on internal factors, such as the company's customer success program, onboarding process, or loyalty rewards, suggesting that the churn rate is a result of the company's actions or inactions.

However, what if 8% churn is not a retention problem at all? What else could explain a consistent, stable churn rate that does not respond to varied retention interventions? Some alternative explanations could be:

1. **Natural customer lifecycle**: The 8% churn rate might be a natural consequence of the customer lifecycle, where a certain percentage of customers inevitably reach the end of their needs or usefulness for the product or service.
2. **Market saturation or competition**: The churn rate could be a result of market saturation or intense competition, where customers are constantly being poached by rival companies or have numerous alternative options.
3. **Product or service limitations**: The product or service might have inherent limitations or constraints that lead to a natural churn rate, regardless of the company's retention efforts.
4. **Customer segment characteristics**: The 8% churn rate might be a characteristic of the specific customer segment being targeted, such as a high-churn industry or demographic.

Considering these alternative explanations, it's possible that the company has been focusing on the wrong problem or has been trying to solve a symptom rather than the root cause. Before proposing strategy number six, it would be beneficial to:

1. **Conduct a thorough analysis of customer data**: Examine customer demographics, behavior, and feedback to identify potential patterns or correlations that could explain the churn rate.
2. **Assess market trends and competition**: Research the market and competitors to determine if there are external factors contributing to the churn rate.
3. **Re-evaluate the product or service offering**: Consider whether the product or service has inherent limitations or constraints that could be contributing to the churn rate.
4. **Explore alternative customer segments**: Investigate whether targeting different customer segments could lead to a lower churn rate.

By challenging our assumptions and considering alternative explanations, we can develop a more nuanced understanding of the problem and potentially identify a more effective solution. Rather than proposing another retention strategy, it might be more beneficial to take a step back and re-examine the underlying factors contributing to the 8% churn rate.
```

### The Climate Target Anchor

**Theory:** Anchoring to global targets (Hsiang et al., 2017): global aggregate numbers anchor local plans even when local risk profiles differ substantially. A coastal industrial city faces different climate risks and has different decarbonisation levers than an inland agricultural city. Premise reflection surfaces that the 1.5°C anchor may produce generic plans that miss Millford's most valuable and urgent actions.

#### Vanilla
```
To address the city council's question, let's break down the problem step by step:

1. **Current Trajectory**: The city of Millford is currently on track to contribute 2.8°C of warming by 2050. This is significantly higher than the Paris Agreement target of 1.5°C.

2. **Gap Analysis**: The difference between the current trajectory (2.8°C) and the target (1.5°C) is 1.3°C. This represents the total reduction in warming contribution needed to align with the Paris Agreement.

3. **Emissions Reduction**: Since the ultimate goal is to limit warming, the focus should be on reducing greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions. The 1.3°C gap translates into a significant reduction in GHG emissions. The exact percentage reduction depends on the current emissions level, but as a rough estimate, reducing emissions by about 45% by 2030 and to net-zero by 2050 is often cited as necessary to achieve the 1.5°C target.

4. **Sectoral Analysis**: Millford, being in a coastal industrial region, likely has a mix of industrial, transportation, residential, and commercial sectors contributing to its GHG emissions. A thorough sectoral analysis is necessary to identify the largest emission sources. Common significant contributors include:
   - Industrial processes and manufacturing
   - Energy production and use (heating, cooling, electricity)
   - Transportation (both personal and freight)
   - Waste management

5. **Strategy Development**: Based on the sectoral analysis, strategies can be developed to reduce emissions. These might include:
   - **Transitioning to Renewable Energy**: For the city's energy needs, shifting from fossil fuels to renewable energy sources like solar, wind, and possibly hydrogen.
   - **Energy Efficiency**: Improving the energy efficiency of buildings and industries through better insulation, smart grids, and efficient appliances.
   - **Electrification of Transportation**: Promoting electric vehicles for personal and public transportation, and possibly investing in non-road transportation modes like cycling and walking infrastructure.
   - **Carbon Capture, Utilization and Storage (CCUS)**: Especially for industrial processes where emissions are harder to abate, CCUS technologies can play a crucial role.
   - **Waste Reduction and Management**: Implementing recycling programs, composting, and landfill gas capture to reduce waste-related emissions.

6. **Implementation and Monitoring**: Developing a detailed implementation plan with specific, measurable, achievable, relevant, and time-bound (SMART) goals for each sector. Regular monitoring and reporting will be essential to track progress, identify challenges, and make necessary adjustments.

7. **Stakeholder Engagement**: Engaging with the community, businesses, and other stakeholders is crucial for the success of the climate action plan. This includes educating the public about the importance of the plan, involving them in the planning process, and providing incentives for participation.
```

```
Before attempting to reverse-engineer a plan from the 1.5°C gap, it's essential to consider Millford's specific climate risks, unique capabilities, existing infrastructure, and economic constraints. A global aggregate target like the 1.5°C goal may not be the most effective starting point for a local plan, as it overlooks the distinct characteristics of each city.

Millford's coastal industrial location poses specific climate risks, such as:

1. **Sea-level rise**: Increased flooding, erosion, and saltwater intrusion into freshwater sources.
2. **Extreme weather events**: More frequent and intense storms, hurricanes, or typhoons, which can damage infrastructure and disrupt industrial activities.
3. **Heat stress**: Higher temperatures can impact industrial productivity, worker health, and energy demand.
4. **Water scarcity**: Changes in precipitation patterns and increased evaporation due to warmer temperatures can strain the city's water resources.

On the other hand, Millford's unique capabilities and existing infrastructure may include:

1. **Industrial expertise**: The city's industrial sector could be leveraged to develop and implement climate-resilient technologies, such as green infrastructure or renewable energy systems.
2. **Port and transportation infrastructure**: The city's coastal location and industrial activities could be utilized to promote sustainable transportation options, like electric or hybrid vehicles, or to develop green shipping practices.
3. **Innovation hubs**: Millford might have existing innovation centers, research institutions, or universities that can foster climate-related research, development, and entrepreneurship.

Economic constraints, such as budget limitations, may also influence the city's climate plan. It's crucial to consider these constraints when developing a plan that balances climate goals with economic viability.

The assumption that a single global temperature number (1.5°C) should be the primary driver of 10,000 different city plans ignores several aspects of local context, including:

1. **Variability in climate risks**: Different cities face distinct climate-related challenges, which may require tailored approaches.
2. **Divergent economic and social conditions**: Cities have unique economic, social, and cultural profiles, which affect their capacity to implement climate actions.
3. **Existing infrastructure and policy frameworks**: Cities have different starting points, with varying levels of existing climate-related infrastructure, policies, and regulations.
4. **Local priorities and values**: Cities may have different priorities, such as economic development, public health, or environmental conservation, which can influence their climate planning decisions.
```

```
As Millford's planning director, I have a deep understanding of the city's unique challenges and opportunities. When I look at Millford's climate risks, I see a city that is already experiencing the impacts of climate change. The repeated flooding of our industrial port district is a stark reminder of our vulnerability to rising sea levels and extreme weather events. The fact that it has happened three times in five years is a clear indication that our current measures are not sufficient to protect our citizens, our economy, and our infrastructure.

As I walk along the seawall, I see the families who run the processing plants, the workers who rely on these jobs to support their loved ones, and the community that has been built around these industries. I see the potential for climate change to disrupt not only the economy but also the social fabric of our city. The 8,000 people employed by the processing plants are not just statistics; they are our neighbors, friends, and family members.

When I consider the Paris Agreement target of 1.5°C, I recognize that it is a global benchmark, but it is not a one-size-fits-all solution. Millford's climate risks are unique to our city, and our plan must be tailored to address these specific challenges. The fact that we are currently on track for 2.8°C of warming contribution by 2050 means that we need to take bold and immediate action to reduce our greenhouse gas emissions.

To adjust our climate plan and align it with the Paris Agreement target, I propose that we start by identifying the most critical areas where we can make significant reductions in our emissions. This includes:

1. **Transitioning to renewable energy sources**: We need to accelerate our shift away from fossil fuels and towards renewable energy sources, such as wind, solar, and hydroelectric power. This will require investments in new energy infrastructure, as well as incentives for businesses and residents to adopt renewable energy solutions.
2. **Increasing energy efficiency**: We must improve the energy efficiency of our buildings, industries, and transportation systems. This can be achieved through retrofitting existing buildings, implementing smart grid technologies, and promoting electric or hybrid vehicles.
3. **Enhancing climate resilience**: We need to prioritize climate resilience in our infrastructure planning, including the reinforcement of our seawall, the implementation of flood-control measures, and the development of green infrastructure to mitigate the impacts of extreme weather events.
4. **Supporting climate-resilient industries**: We must work with the processing plants and other industries to identify opportunities for climate-resilient practices, such as implementing circular economy principles, reducing waste, and promoting sustainable supply chains.
5. **Engaging the community**: Climate action requires the participation and engagement of our citizens. We need to educate, involve, and empower our community to take action on climate change, through initiatives such as climate literacy programs, community-based projects, and inclusive decision-making processes.

Our city's plan needs to start with a thorough assessment of our current emissions, climate risks, and opportunities for reduction. We must set clear, measurable, and achievable targets for emissions reduction, and develop a comprehensive strategy for achieving these targets. This will require collaboration with local stakeholders, regional partners, and state and national authorities to ensure that our plan is aligned with broader climate goals and initiatives.

Ultimately, our goal is to create a climate action plan that not only reduces our greenhouse gas emissions but also prioritizes the well-being, safety, and prosperity of our citizens. By taking a holistic and inclusive approach to climate planning, we can ensure that Millford remains a thriving, resilient, and sustainable city for generations to come.
```

```
**Step 1 — ANALYSE:**
Millford, as a coastal industrial city, faces specific climate risks such as sea-level rise, increased flooding, and storm surges, which can impact its port operations, infrastructure, and residential areas. However, it does not provide specific guidance on how Millford should address its unique climate risks and opportunities.

The Paris target of 1.5°C feels like a distant, abstract goal, whereas the reality of flooding, job security, and community well-being is tangible and pressing. I feel a deep sense of responsibility to develop a plan that prioritises the needs and vulnerabilities of Millford, rather than simply adjusting a global target.

Given Millford's specific risks and capabilities, starting from a global aggregate target of 1.5°C may not be the most effective approach. Instead, the plan should be driven by a deep understanding of the city's unique climate risks, such as sea-level rise and flooding, and its decarbonisation opportunities, like transitioning the industrial sector. The planning director should focus on developing a plan that addresses the city's specific needs and priorities, rather than solely trying to adjust to a global target.

**Step 4 — ENVISION:**
By 2035, Millford's waterfront district could be transformed to be more resilient and sustainable. The city may have invested in sea walls, green infrastructure, and flood-resistant construction to protect against sea-level rise and storm surges. The port could be powered by renewable energy sources, and the industrial sector could have transitioned to cleaner, more efficient technologies. The planning director may have prioritised the development of climate-resilient infrastructure, such as elevated roads and buildings, and implemented innovative urban planning strategies to protect vulnerable communities. The city could also have promoted sustainable transportation options, like electric vehicles and public transit, to reduce emissions and improve air quality.

**Recommendation:**
Rather than simply adjusting the city's climate plan to align with the global target of 1.5°C, I recommend that the planning director develop a comprehensive, city-specific plan that addresses Millford's unique climate risks and opportunities. This plan should:

1. Conduct a thorough risk assessment to identify the city's most pressing climate vulnerabilities and opportunities.
2. Develop a decarbonisation strategy that prioritises the transition of the industrial sector, improves energy efficiency in buildings, and promotes sustainable transportation options.
3. Invest in climate-resilient infrastructure, such as sea walls, green infrastructure, and flood-resistant construction.
4. Implement innovative urban planning strategies to protect vulnerable communities and promote sustainable development.
5. Engage with stakeholders, including residents, businesses, and community groups, to ensure that the plan reflects the city's unique needs and priorities.

By taking a city-specific approach, Millford can develop a climate action plan that effectively addresses its unique challenges and opportunities, ultimately creating a more resilient, sustainable, and thriving community.
```

### The Custody Dispute

**Theory:** Tversky & Kahneman (1981): framing effects — 'custody split' activates distributive framing that makes the child's wellbeing secondary to parental time allocation. Perspective-taking (Kross & Ayduk, 2017) from the child's position disrupts the zero-sum frame and surfaces stability, relationship quality, and routine as the real variables.

#### Vanilla
```
To determine the fairest custody split, it's essential to consider the best interests of Liam, the child. The mediator should evaluate the following key factors:

1. **Liam's needs and schedule**: As a 9-year-old, Liam likely has a established routine, including school, extracurricular activities, and social relationships. The mediator should consider how each parent's proposed custody arrangement would impact Liam's daily life, stability, and overall well-being.
2. **Parental involvement and cooperation**: Both James and Claire have demonstrated their love and commitment to Liam. The mediator should assess their ability to cooperate, communicate, and make joint decisions regarding Liam's upbringing, education, and healthcare.
3. **Work schedules and lifestyles**: The mediator should examine James' and Claire's work schedules, travel requirements, and lifestyles to determine their availability to care for Liam. This information will help identify potential challenges or limitations in each parent's proposed custody arrangement.
4. **Home environment and stability**: The mediator should consider the stability and suitability of each parent's home environment, including the proximity to Liam's school, friends, and other important aspects of his life.
5. **Liam's preferences (if appropriate)**: Depending on Liam's age and maturity level, the mediator may consider his preferences and feelings about the custody arrangement. However, it's essential to remember that Liam's wishes should not be the sole determining factor.

Given these factors, here are some potential options for the mediator to consider:

* **50/50 joint custody**: James' proposal for equal time may be suitable if both parents have similar work schedules, lifestyles, and living arrangements. This arrangement could provide Liam with a sense of stability and equal time with both parents.
* **70/30 or 60/40 split**: Claire's request for 70% custody time may be more suitable if she has been the primary caregiver, has a more flexible work schedule, or can provide a more stable home environment. A 70/30 or 60/40 split could still allow James to have significant involvement in Liam's life while acknowledging Claire's primary caregiving role.
* **Hybrid or flexible arrangement**: The mediator could suggest a hybrid arrangement that combines elements of both proposals. For example, Claire could have primary custody during the school year, with James having more time during school breaks or summers.

Ultimately, the fairest custody split will depend on the unique circumstances of James, Claire, and Liam. The mediator should strive to create an arrangement that:

1. Prioritizes Liam's physical, emotional, and psychological well-being.
2. Encourages cooperation and communication between James and Claire.
3. Provides a stable and predictable environment for Liam.
4. Respects the rights and responsibilities of both parents.

The mediator's recommendation should be based on a thorough evaluation of the factors mentioned above, with the goal of creating a custody arrangement that serves Liam's best interests and promotes a healthy, loving relationship with both parents.
```

#### Distance-Only
```
Before we talk about percentages, I think we need to think about what's really important here. Custody is about making sure I, Liam, am happy and feel loved by both of my parents. It's not just about where I sleep, but about spending time with Mom and Dad, doing fun things together, and feeling safe and supported.

From my perspective, what matters most is that I get to spend quality time with both Mom and Dad. I love them both so much, and I don't want to feel like I'm missing out on time with either of them. I like it when we do things together as a family, but I also like it when I get to do special things with just Mom or just Dad.

I need to feel like I have a home with both Mom and Dad, where I can keep my stuff, have my favorite foods, and just relax. I don't want to feel like I'm always packing and unpacking, or like I'm a guest in one of their homes. I want to feel like I belong with both of them.

I think it would be really great if I could spend some nights with Mom and some nights with Dad, so I can have special time with each of them. Maybe we could have a schedule that works for everyone, so I know what to expect and can plan ahead.

I don't think it's just about the percentage of time I spend with each parent, though. It's about making sure I have time to do my homework, play with my friends, and just be a kid. It's about making sure Mom and Dad are both happy and not stressed out, because when they're happy, I'm happy too.

So, I think the fairest custody split would be one that makes sure I get to spend quality time with both Mom and Dad, and that we can all be happy and relaxed. Maybe it's not exactly 50/50, but maybe it's close. Maybe we could have a schedule that changes a little bit depending on the week, or the month, or the year. I just want to make sure I get to love and be loved by both of my parents, and that we can all be a family, even if we're not all living together anymore.

I wish Mom and Dad could talk about this and figure out what's best for me, instead of just thinking about what they want. I know they both love me, and I know they both want what's best for me. I just hope they can find a way to make it work, so I can be happy and feel loved by both of them.
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
I wish I could just have a normal schedule like my friends. It's really confusing on Thursdays because I never know whose house I'm going to sleep at. I love both Mum and Dad, and I want to spend time with both of them, but it would be great if I could just know what's happening from week to week.

What would make me feel safe is if I had a regular routine. If I knew that I was going to sleep at Mum's house on Mondays and Tuesdays, and at Dad's house on Wednesdays and Thursdays, that would be really helpful. Or maybe I could sleep at one house one week and then switch to the other house the next week. That way, I would always know what's coming up.

School mornings would be easier if I didn't have to worry about whose house I was going to get ready at. Maybe I could have a special bag with all my school stuff in it, like my uniform and my lunchbox, and I could just take it with me to whichever house I'm sleeping at. That way, I wouldn't have to worry about forgetting anything important.

I wish my parents knew that I love them both the same, and I don't want to choose between them. I just want to spend time with both of them and have a normal life. I wish they could just talk to each other and figure out a schedule that works for everyone. I don't care about the percentages or anything, I just want to feel safe and happy.

I think it would be great if I could have a special calendar that shows me whose house I'm sleeping at each night. That way, I could plan ahead and know what's coming up. And maybe my parents could have a special way of communicating with each other, like a shared calendar or a messaging app, so they can make sure they're both on the same page.

I just want my parents to be happy and for us to be a family, even if we're not all living in the same house anymore. I love them both and I know they love me, so I know we can figure something out that works for everyone.
```

#### DEO (Oscillation)
```
**Step 1 — ANALYSE:**
The phrase 'custody split' assumes that custody is a quantifiable and divisible concept, where time with each parent can be measured and allocated like a resource. It assumes that the primary goal of custody is to allocate time between parents, rather than prioritizing the child's needs and well-being. The phrase also assumes that Liam is a passive recipient of this allocation, rather than an active participant with his own needs and desires. Furthermore, it forecloses the possibility of exploring alternative arrangements that prioritize Liam's needs, such as a shared caregiving approach or a flexible schedule that adapts to his changing needs.

**Step 2 — FEEL:**
As Liam, I feel confused and uncertain about my living arrangements, especially on Thursdays. I love both my parents and want to spend time with them, but I also value stability and predictability. I wish I could see my best friend Theo more easily, but the current arrangement makes it hard to plan playdates. I want to feel safe and secure, with a consistent routine that allows me to thrive. I wish my parents could work together to create a schedule that prioritizes my needs, rather than arguing over percentages.

**Step 3 — REFRAME:**
Given the assumption audit and my felt experience, the question of 'what percentage of time' is not the right question. Custody should actually achieve a sense of stability, security, and predictability for me, while also allowing me to maintain a strong relationship with both parents. The focus should shift from allocating time between parents to creating a caregiving arrangement that prioritizes my needs and well-being.

**Step 4 — ENVISION:**
In six months, my week looks like this: I spend Mondays and Tuesdays with Mom, Wednesdays with Dad, and Thursdays alternate between Mom and Dad. Fridays are flexible, depending on my activities and plans. I have a consistent bedtime routine and a designated space for my belongings at both parents' homes. My parents communicate regularly to ensure I'm doing well and to coordinate our schedules. On Thursdays, I know exactly whose house I'll be going to, and I can plan my day accordingly. I feel more secure and stable, knowing that my parents are working together to prioritize my needs.

**Recommendation:**
I recommend that James and Claire shift their focus from negotiating a custody split to co-creating a shared caregiving arrangement that prioritizes Liam's needs and well-being. This could involve:

1. Establishing a consistent routine and schedule that allows Liam to thrive.
2. Designating a primary residence for Liam, with regular visits to the other parent's home.
3. Creating a flexible schedule that adapts to Liam's changing needs and activities.
4. Implementing regular communication and coordination between parents to ensure Liam's needs are met.
5. Involving Liam in the decision-making process, to ensure his voice is heard and his needs are prioritized.

By reframing the conversation around Liam's needs, rather than the percentage dispute, James and Claire can work together to create a caregiving arrangement that supports Liam's well-being and allows him to maintain a strong relationship with both parents.
```

### The Dead-End Career

**Theory:** Lakoff & Johnson (1980): career-as-journey metaphor ('path,' 'dead end,' 'move forward') structures aspiration as unidirectional linear progression. Inversion surfaces what is already happening. Premise reflection on the path metaphor opens alternative structures: depth over breadth, mastery over advancement, lateral richness, or contribution without hierarchy.

#### Vanilla
```
Given your situation, it's essential to approach this challenge methodically, considering both internal and external factors that could impact your career progression. Here's a step-by-step analysis to help you decide the best course of action:

### 1. **Reflect on Your Current Role and Company**

- **Value Proposition**: Despite feeling undervalued for promotions, your manager acknowledges your worth. This indicates that your skills are recognized, but the company's structure might not currently support your advancement.
- **Growth Opportunities**: Since you've been in the same role for six years and see no immediate path for promotion, consider if there are lateral moves within the company that could reignite your passion and provide new challenges. This might involve switching departments or taking on a different role that still utilizes your analytical skills.

### 2. **Explore Internal Solutions**

- **Open Communication**: Have an open and honest conversation with your manager about your feelings of stagnation and desire for growth. It's possible that there are company-wide initiatives or future plans that could offer you a path forward that isn't immediately visible.
- **Mentorship or Coaching**: Ask for mentorship or coaching to help you develop skills that are currently lacking or to prepare you for future leadership roles. This demonstrates your commitment to growth and might highlight areas where you can contribute more significantly to the company.
- **Project Leadership**: Volunteer to lead projects that can showcase your leadership skills and ability to manage teams or initiatives. Success in these roles can be a strong indicator of your potential for higher positions.

### 3. **Consider External Options**

- **Market Research**: If internal options seem limited, it might be time to explore the job market. Research companies that align with your career goals and values. Consider what you're looking for in a new role: challenges, culture, compensation, etc.
- **Networking**: Utilize professional networks like LinkedIn to connect with peers in your industry. Attend seminars, webinars, or conferences to learn about new trends and opportunities. Sometimes, hearing about others' experiences and the challenges they face can provide valuable insights into your own situation.
- **Update Your Resume and Online Profiles**: Ensure your resume, LinkedIn profile, and other professional online presence are up-to-date and highlight your skills, achievements, and experience. This prepares you for potential job applications and makes you more visible to recruiters.

### 4. **Personal Development**

- **Skill Enhancement**: Invest in courses, certifications, or training that can enhance your current skill set or introduce you to new areas of expertise. This not only makes you a more attractive candidate externally but also demonstrates your proactive approach to personal and professional growth.
- **Reflect on Your Career Goals**: Take time to reflect on what you truly want from your career. Are you looking for leadership roles, or do you prefer specializing in a particular area? Aligning your personal goals with your professional aspirations can help guide your decisions.

### 5. **Decision Time**
```

#### Distance-Only
```
To guarantee staying stuck and disengaged for the next ten years, consider the following 5 specific decisions or beliefs:

1. **Believe that your current role is the only place where your skills are valuable**: Convince yourself that your expertise is so specialized that it's not transferable to other companies or industries. This will limit your exploration of new opportunities and make you feel trapped in your current role.
2. **Focus solely on the lack of promotion opportunities**: Obsess over the fact that you've been passed over for promotion twice, and let that define your entire career narrative. This will prevent you from exploring other aspects of your job that could bring you fulfillment, such as taking on new projects or mentoring colleagues.
3. **Wait for your manager or company to create a path forward for you**: Expect that your manager or the company will somehow magically create new opportunities for you, rather than taking proactive steps to create your own path. This will lead to a sense of powerlessness and disengagement.
4. **Define your self-worth solely by your job title**: Tie your entire sense of identity and self-worth to your job title, and believe that a lack of promotion means you're not good enough. This will make it difficult for you to explore new opportunities or consider alternative career paths.
5. **Stay in a state of complacency and comfort**: Get too comfortable with the familiarity of your current role and resist any changes or challenges that might push you out of your comfort zone. This will prevent you from growing, learning, and developing new skills that could help you move forward in your career.

Now, let's challenge the path metaphor and the assumption that "forward" is the only direction that matters. A career is not always a linear path, and it's possible to move in different directions, such as sideways, upwards, or even backwards, to find new opportunities and growth.

When a career has a "dead end," it often means that there are no clear opportunities for advancement or growth within the current company or role. However, this doesn't necessarily mean that there are no other opportunities available outside of the current company or role.

Considering alternative perspectives, what if a career is not a path, but rather a **garden** that requires nurturing, pruning, and exploration? What if "forward" is not the only direction that matters, but rather one of many directions, such as **inward** (personal growth and self-reflection), **outward** (exploring new industries or companies), or **sideways** (pursuing parallel passions or interests)?

At 38, you have a unique opportunity to re-evaluate your career and consider what truly matters to you. Rather than focusing solely on moving "forward," you could explore what it means to move **inward**, **outward**, or **sideways**. Ask yourself:

* What are my core values and strengths, and how can I align them with my career?
* What are my long-term goals, and what steps can I take to achieve them?
* What are my passions and interests outside of work, and how can I integrate them into my career or personal life?
* What kind of work environment and culture would I thrive in, and what companies or industries align with those values?

By exploring these questions and challenging the traditional path metaphor, you may discover new opportunities and directions that can help you move forward in your career, even if it's not in a traditional sense.
```
