### The Salary Negotiation Deadlock

**Theory:** Fisher & Ury (1981): positional bargaining collapses to the single dimension of salary. Frame-naming reveals the shared assumption that compensation = dollars. Distant analogy from gift/barter economies (Mauss, 1925) surfaces multi-dimensional value exchange — non-monetary compensation expands the solution space beyond the $95K-$120K corridor.

#### Vanilla
```
To resolve this negotiation and keep Marcus, let's break down the key factors involved and weigh the options carefully.

**Key Factors:**

1. **Marcus's market value**: He believes his skills and experience warrant a salary of $120,000, which is supported by market benchmarks.
2. **Company's salary band cap**: HR has a firm cap of $95,000 for Marcus's role, which is significantly lower than his requested salary.
3. **Manager's goal**: The manager wants to keep Marcus, indicating that he values Marcus's contributions to the company.
4. **Negotiation dynamics**: Both sides feel their position is reasonable, which can create a stalemate.

**Options:**

1. **Meet Marcus's requested salary**: The company could offer Marcus the $120,000 he's asking for, which would likely keep him happy and motivated. However, this would require HR to make an exception to the salary band cap, which might set a precedent for future negotiations.
2. **Offer a compromise**: The company could propose a salary between $95,000 and $120,000, such as $105,000 or $110,000. This would show that the company is willing to listen to Marcus's concerns and work towards a mutually beneficial solution.
3. **Provide non-monetary benefits**: The company could offer additional benefits, such as a bonus structure, extra vacation days, or professional development opportunities, to offset the difference between the offered salary and Marcus's requested salary.
4. **Explore alternative solutions**: The company could consider promoting Marcus to a higher role or creating a new position that would allow him to earn a higher salary.

**Recommendation:**

Considering the factors and options, I recommend a hybrid approach:

1. **Offer a compromise salary**: The company could propose a salary of $105,000, which is still below Marcus's requested salary but closer to his market value.
2. **Provide non-monetary benefits**: In addition to the compromised salary, the company could offer a bonus structure, such as a 10% annual bonus, or extra vacation days to demonstrate their commitment to Marcus's well-being and job satisfaction.
3. **Create a performance-based plan**: The company could establish a performance-based plan that would allow Marcus to earn additional income or benefits based on his achievements and contributions to the company. This would align his compensation with his value to the organization.
4. **Review and revise the salary band cap**: The company should review their salary band cap and consider revising it to reflect market conditions and the value of their employees. This would help prevent similar negotiations in the future and ensure that the company can attract and retain top talent.
```

```
Both Marcus and the company assume that compensation means a monetary salary, specifically a yearly dollar amount, that reflects the value of Marcus' work and contributions to the company.

To find an analogy, let's consider the gift economy of the indigenous Tlingit people of the Pacific Northwest Coast of North America. In their traditional potlatch system, value exchange goes beyond a single currency. The potlatch is a ceremonial exchange of gifts, goods, and services, where the value of an item is not solely determined by its monetary worth. Instead, the value is tied to the item's cultural significance, the status of the giver and receiver, and the relationships between them.

The structural pattern of this exchange can be broken down into several key elements:

1. **Multi-dimensional value**: The value of a gift or service is not limited to its monetary equivalent. Instead, it encompasses social, cultural, and relational aspects.
2. **Reciprocity**: The exchange is based on reciprocity, where the giver expects something in return, not necessarily of equal monetary value, but of equal social and cultural significance.
3. **Relationship-building**: The exchange strengthens social bonds and reinforces relationships between individuals and groups.
4. **Non-monetary benefits**: The exchange can include non-monetary benefits, such as prestige, status, and access to knowledge, networks, or resources.

Applying this pattern to the employment relationship between Marcus and the company, we can consider a compromise that goes beyond a single monetary salary. Here are some potential elements:

1. **Multi-dimensional value**: Instead of focusing solely on the dollar amount, the company could offer a package that includes non-monetary benefits, such as:
	* Additional vacation days or flexible work arrangements, which could be valued at $5,000 to $10,000 per year.

	* A 10% bonus for meeting performance targets, which could be valued at $9,500 per year.

3. **Relationship-building**: The company could offer a more personalized and supportive work environment, such as:
```

### The Scale Assumption

**Theory:** Mezirow (1990): uncritically acquired assumptions ('NoSQL is for scale') function as frames. The premise that 10K DAU requires a database migration is itself the lock-in. Decomposing 'slow' into specific symptoms reveals the real problem is almost certainly not the database engine.

#### Vanilla
```
To address the issue of increasing query response times in a PostgreSQL database, let's consider the key factors and options involved.

**Current Situation:**

1. **PostgreSQL Database:** The current database management system in use.
2. **10,000 Daily Active Users:** The number of users interacting with the database daily.
3. **Increasing Query Response Times:** Query response times have increased from 50ms to 800ms over the past 3 months.

**Options:**

1. **MongoDB:** A NoSQL, document-oriented database management system.
2. **Cassandra:** A NoSQL, distributed database management system designed for handling large amounts of data across many commodity servers.

**Factors to Consider:**

1. **Data Structure:** What type of data is being stored? Is it structured, semi-structured, or unstructured?
2. **Scalability:** How will the chosen database management system handle increased traffic and data growth?
3. **Query Patterns:** What types of queries are being executed? Are they simple, complex, or a mix of both?
4. **Data Relationships:** Are there complex relationships between data entities that need to be maintained?
5. **ACID Compliance:** Is Atomicity, Consistency, Isolation, and Durability (ACID) compliance required for database transactions?
6. **Development Team Expertise:** What is the level of expertise of the development team with the proposed database management systems?

**Analysis:**

1. **PostgreSQL:** Before considering a migration to a different database management system, it's essential to investigate the root cause of the increasing query response times. This could be due to various factors such as:

*[... truncated, 57 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
### Analyzing the Situation

To determine if PostgreSQL is indeed the bottleneck and whether migration to another database is necessary, let's break down the key questions:

1. **Is PostgreSQL actually the bottleneck? 10,000 DAU translates to roughly how many queries per second?**

   The number of daily active users (DAU) alone doesn't directly translate to queries per second (QPS) without knowing the usage patterns of your application. However, we can make an educated estimate. Assuming an average user interacts with your application 10 times a day (a conservative estimate, as many applications see much higher interaction rates), you'd have approximately 100,000 interactions per day. Spread over 24 hours (86,400 seconds), this gives you about 1.16 interactions per second.

   If each interaction results in at least one query (again, a conservative estimate, as many interactions can result in multiple queries), you're looking at roughly 1-2 queries per second (QPS) as a very rough estimate. However, this can vary widely based on the application's functionality and user behavior. For a more accurate assessment, you should monitor your database's actual query load.

2. **Decompose 'slow' into specifics: which queries are slow? What changed in the past 3 months? Is it all queries or specific patterns?**

   The increase in query response times from 50ms to 800ms over three months indicates a significant performance degradation. To address this, you need to identify:
   - **Specific slow queries:** Use PostgreSQL's built-in tools like `pg_stat_statements` or external tools to log and analyze slow queries. This will help pinpoint which queries are causing the bottleneck.
   - **Changes over the past 3 months:** Consider any application updates, changes in user behavior, or increases in data volume that could be contributing to the slowdown. Perhaps new features were added, leading to more complex queries or increased traffic.
   - **Patterns of slowness:** Determine if the slowness is consistent across all queries and times of the day or if there are specific patterns (e.g., certain times of the day, specific query types).

3. **What would have to be true for PostgreSQL to be unable to handle this load?**

   For PostgreSQL to be genuinely unable to handle the load, several conditions would need to be met:
   - **Insufficient resources:** The server running PostgreSQL lacks sufficient CPU, RAM, or disk I/O capacity to handle the query load efficiently.
   - **Inefficient database design:** The database schema is poorly optimized for the queries being run, leading to inefficient data retrieval and manipulation.
   - **Lack of indexing:** Critical columns used in WHERE, JOIN, and ORDER BY clauses lack appropriate indexes, causing PostgreSQL to perform full table scans unnecessarily.
   - **Inadequate maintenance:** Failure to regularly update statistics, vacuum tables, or perform other necessary maintenance tasks could lead to performance degradation.


*[... truncated, 36 lines total]*
```

```
The frustration is palpable. As I stare at the database logs, I see a significant increase in query execution time, with some queries taking up to 800ms to complete. This is a far cry from the 50ms response times we used to enjoy. I notice that the slow queries are mostly related to our most popular features, such as user profiles and activity feeds.

Upon further investigation, I see that our database has grown significantly over the past three months. We've added new features, and our user base has expanded, resulting in a substantial increase in data volume. I also notice that our indexing strategy, which was adequate when we had fewer users, is no longer sufficient to handle the increased load.

Three months ago, when this issue started, we launched a new feature that allows users to upload and share multimedia content. This feature has been incredibly popular, and as a result, our database has seen a massive influx of new data. I suspect that this new feature, combined with the growing user base, has put a significant strain on our PostgreSQL database.

My gut tells me that the issue is not necessarily with the database technology itself, but rather with how we're using it. I think we've outgrown our current database schema and indexing strategy, and we need to reassess our data modeling and querying approaches. Before considering a migration to a new database technology like MongoDB or Cassandra, I would like to investigate potential optimizations to our existing PostgreSQL database.

Some questions I have include:

* Are our queries optimized, or are we using inefficient queries that are causing the slowdown?
* Are our indexes adequate, or do we need to re-evaluate our indexing strategy to better support our growing data volume?
* Are we experiencing any locking or contention issues due to increased concurrent access to our database?
* Have we properly configured our PostgreSQL instance for optimal performance, or are there tuning opportunities that we've missed?

Only after thoroughly investigating these questions and optimizing our existing database infrastructure would I consider migrating to a new database technology like MongoDB or Cassandra. Even then, I would want to carefully evaluate the trade-offs and ensure that the new technology aligns with our specific use case and performance requirements.

In particular, I would consider the following factors when evaluating MongoDB and Cassandra:

* MongoDB:
	+ Pros: flexible schema, high performance for document-oriented data, ease of use.
	+ Cons: potential issues with data consistency and availability, may not be suitable for very large datasets.
* Cassandra:
	+ Pros: highly scalable, fault-tolerant, and designed for large, distributed datasets.
	+ Cons: steeper learning curve, may require significant changes to our application architecture.
```

```
To address the issue of slow query response times in the PostgreSQL database, let's break down the problem and follow the steps outlined:

### Step 1 — DECOMPOSE

- **Understanding 'slow':** The term 'slow' refers to the significant increase in query response times from 50ms to 800ms over the past 3 months.
- **Queries per second for 10,000 DAU:** To estimate the queries per second, we need to know the average number of queries per user per second. Assuming an average of 1 query per user per 10 seconds (a rough estimate), with 10,000 DAU, we have approximately 1,000 queries per second (10,000 users / 10 seconds per query).
- **Specific slow queries:** Without specific details, it's hard to pinpoint which queries are slow. However, common culprits include queries with poor indexing, those that perform full table scans, or complex joins.
- **Changes in the past 3 months:** Possible changes include an increase in data volume, alterations in the database schema, shifts in traffic patterns, or updates to the application code that could be causing inefficient queries.

### Step 2 — FEEL

As the developer who built this app, staring at the logs, I might see:
- **Increased load:** More users or more queries per user.
- **New or changed queries:** Recent code updates might have introduced inefficient queries or increased the load on existing ones.
- **Lack of indexing or improper indexing:** Queries might not be using indexes effectively, leading to full table scans.
- **Data distribution:** Skewed data distribution could lead to hotspots in the database, slowing down certain queries.

My instinct before considering MongoDB or Cassandra would be to investigate recent code changes, check database indexing, and look into query optimization techniques.

### Step 3 — REFRAME

Given the decomposition and the developer's instinct, the question 'MongoDB vs Cassandra' might not be the right one. The real question should be: **What is causing the slowdown in our PostgreSQL database, and how can we optimize it?**

This involves examining recent changes, optimizing queries, ensuring proper indexing, and possibly scaling the database vertically (more powerful hardware) or horizontally (more servers).
```

### The Shipping Container Problem

**Theory:** McCaffrey (2012): functional labels ('container') activate associated functions ('contain things / be a room') and obscure generic properties (corrugated steel panels, I-beam frames, plywood sheets). Stripping labels reveals the components are building materials, not just rooms.

#### Vanilla
```
To address the disaster relief organization's need for temporary emergency housing, let's break down the problem into key factors and weigh the options carefully.

**Key Factors:**

1. **Time constraint:** The organization has only 72 hours to build temporary housing for 500 people.
2. **Available resources:** 200 empty steel shipping containers, each 40 feet long.
3. **Space requirements:** The organization needs to accommodate 500 people.
4. **Safety and comfort:** The temporary housing should provide a safe and relatively comfortable environment for the occupants.

**Options:**

1. **Use containers as individual units:** Each container could be converted into a separate living unit. However, this would only provide housing for 200 people, leaving 300 people without shelter.
2. **Stack containers:** Containers could be stacked on top of each other to increase the overall living space. However, this would require additional support structures and safety measures to ensure stability and prevent accidents.
3. **Join containers together:** Containers could be joined together to create larger living units. This would allow for more efficient use of space and provide a more communal living environment.
4. **Modify containers:** Containers could be modified to include essential amenities such as windows, doors, lighting, and ventilation. This would improve the living conditions but might require additional materials and labor.

**Recommendation:**

To address the organization's needs, I recommend a combination of options 3 and 4: joining containers together and modifying them to create larger, more comfortable living units.

**Step-by-Step Plan:**

1. **Sort and prepare containers:** Inspect the containers for any damage and prepare them for use. Clean and disinfect the containers to ensure a safe environment.
```

```
To shelter the most people in the time we have, I would prioritize the following:

* **Speed over comfort**: Focus on providing basic shelter and amenities, rather than trying to create comfortable or luxurious living spaces.
* **Efficient use of resources**: Utilize the containers and available resources in a way that maximizes the number of people we can shelter, rather than trying to create individualized or customized spaces.
```

### The Sinking Ship

**Theory:** Lakoff & Johnson (1980): conceptual metaphors ('company is a ship') structure thought — the metaphor activates sinking-ship schemas (plug leaks, bail water, abandon ship) and forecloses others. Decomposition to literal facts strips the narrative lock-in. Each 'bad' event may contain signal: self-selecting exits, client pressure prompting a needed pivot.

#### Vanilla
```
Given the current situation, it's essential to approach this problem with a clear and level head, considering both short-term and long-term implications. Here's a step-by-step analysis of the key factors and potential recommendations:

**Assess the Current Situation:**

1. **Burn rate:** The company is burning through its runway faster than projected, indicating a potential issue with cost management or revenue growth.
2. **Talent loss:** The resignation of two best engineers to a competitor is a significant blow, as it not only affects the company's technical capabilities but also sends a negative signal to other employees and potential hires.
3. **Client loss:** The biggest client (32% of revenue) evaluating alternatives is a significant risk, as losing this client could severely impact the company's revenue and viability.
4. **Cash reserves:** With eight months of cash left, the company has a limited window to turn things around.

**Short-Term Priorities (0-3 months):**

1. **Cost reduction:** Implement immediate cost-cutting measures to reduce the burn rate, such as:
	* Renegotiating contracts with vendors and suppliers.
	* Reducing non-essential expenses (e.g., travel, entertainment).
	* Freezing hiring and minimizing personnel costs.
2. **Client retention:** Focus on retaining the biggest client by:
	* Assigning a dedicated team to ensure their needs are met.
	* Offering customized solutions or discounts to demonstrate value.
	* Regularly communicating with the client to address concerns and provide updates.
3. **Talent acquisition:** Act quickly to replace the lost engineers and prevent further brain drain by:
	* Offering competitive salaries and benefits to attract top talent.
	* Leveraging professional networks and referrals to find suitable candidates.
	* Considering interim or contract workers to fill critical gaps.

**Mid-Term Strategies (3-6 months):**

*[... truncated, 56 lines total]*
```

```
Here are the events described in literal, factual terms:

1. The company is burning through its runway faster than projected.
	* This means that the company's expenses are higher than expected, resulting in a faster depletion of its available funds.
	* This creates the option to review and adjust the company's budget, and it closes the option to continue operating at the current expense level.
2. Two of the company's best engineers resigned to join a competitor.
	* This means that the company has lost two skilled employees, which may impact its ability to develop and deliver products or services.
	* This creates the option to recruit and hire new engineers, and it closes the option to rely on the expertise and experience of the two departed engineers.
3. The company's biggest client (32% of revenue) is evaluating alternatives.
	* This means that the client is considering switching to a different supplier or vendor, which could result in a significant loss of revenue for the company.
	* This creates the option to negotiate with the client to retain their business, and it closes the option to assume that the client's business is secure.
4. The company has eight months of cash left.
	* This means that the company has a limited amount of time to address its financial situation before it runs out of funds.
	* This creates the option to develop a plan to reduce expenses, increase revenue, or secure additional funding, and it closes the option to delay addressing the company's financial situation.

Is this company actually "sinking," or is something else happening that the metaphor is preventing us from seeing clearly? The facts suggest that the company is facing significant challenges, including a faster-than-expected burn rate, loss of key talent, and potential loss of a major client. However, the company still has eight months of cash left, which provides a window of opportunity to address these challenges and develop a plan to get back on track.

Rather than being a "sinking ship," the company may be facing a critical inflection point, where it needs to make significant adjustments to its business model, operations, or strategy in order to survive and thrive. The metaphor of a "sinking ship" may be obscuring other potential solutions or perspectives, such as:

* The company's burn rate may be a result of over-investment in certain areas, and adjusting the budget could help to extend the company's runway.
* The loss of key engineers may create an opportunity to re-evaluate the company's talent needs and develop a more effective recruitment and retention strategy.
* The potential loss of a major client may prompt the company to diversify its revenue streams and reduce its dependence on a single client.
* The company's financial situation may be a result of external factors, such as market changes or economic conditions, which could be addressed through strategic partnerships, acquisitions, or other business development initiatives.

By stripping away the metaphor and focusing on the facts, it becomes clear that the company is facing significant challenges, but it is not necessarily doomed to "sink." Instead, it has a limited time to address its issues and develop a plan to get back on track, which could involve a range of strategic and operational adjustments.
```

### The Standardise vs. Customise Tension

**Theory:** Tversky & Kahneman (1981): binary framing (standardise vs. customise) forecloses the design space. Fashion analogy (Gentner, 2003) provides a structural model with three positions rather than two — made-to-measure maps to standardised methodology with client-specific configuration, a position that may defend premium pricing while reducing delivery cost.

#### Vanilla
```
To address this dilemma, let's break down the key factors and considerations involved:

1. **Pinnacle's Current Model**: Pinnacle Management Consulting has built its reputation on bespoke strategy engagements, which are custom-designed for each client. This model has presumably allowed Pinnacle to differentiate itself and charge premium prices ($250,000-$500,000 per engagement) due to the high value clients place on tailored solutions.

2. **Competitor Entry**: The entry of two large competitors offering standardized consulting packages at significantly lower prices ($50,000-$80,000) poses a direct challenge to Pinnacle's business model. These competitors are attracting clients who are sensitive to price, which could erode Pinnacle's client base.

3. **Considerations for Standardization**:
   - **Pro**: Standardizing offerings could make Pinnacle more competitive in terms of pricing, potentially allowing it to retain or regain market share. It could also streamline operations, reducing the time and cost associated with designing completely bespoke solutions for each client.
   - **Con**: Standardization might dilute Pinnacle's unique selling proposition (USP) and the premium value it offers. Clients who are currently willing to pay for bespoke services might perceive a standardized product as less valuable, potentially leading to a loss of high-paying clients. Additionally, standardization could force Pinnacle to compete more directly on price, which might not be sustainable or profitable in the long term.

4. **Considerations for Maintaining the Custom Model**:
   - **Pro**: Sticking to the bespoke model allows Pinnacle to maintain its premium brand image and continue to attract clients who value highly customized solutions. This approach enables Pinnacle to differentiate itself from the competitors and potentially maintain its pricing power.
   - **Con**: Refusing to adapt to market changes could lead to a decline in market share as price-sensitive clients opt for the cheaper, standardized solutions offered by competitors.

5. **Hybrid Approach**: A potential middle ground could be to offer a tiered service model. This would involve maintaining the bespoke, high-end service for clients who value and can afford it, while also introducing a more standardized, lower-cost option for clients with less complex needs or tighter budgets. This approach would allow Pinnacle to address the competitive threat while preserving its premium brand and revenue streams.

**Recommendation**:
Given the considerations above, the best approach for Pinnacle Management Consulting would likely be to adopt a hybrid model. This involves:

1. **Maintaining the Bespoke Service**: Continue to offer high-end, custom-designed strategy engagements for clients who value and are willing to pay for tailored solutions. This preserves Pinnacle's premium brand image and revenue streams from high-paying clients.

2. **Introducing a Standardized Option**: Develop a standardized consulting package that can be offered at a lower price point, targeting clients with less complex needs or those who are more price-sensitive. This would help Pinnacle compete in the mid-tier market and prevent further erosion of its client base to competitors.

3. **Clearly Differentiate the Offerings**: It's crucial to clearly differentiate the bespoke and standardized services in terms of value proposition, outcomes, and pricing. This differentiation will help manage client expectations and prevent cannibalization of the premium service by the lower-cost option.


*[... truncated, 28 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
To determine whether Pinnacle Management Consulting should standardize its offerings or stay with its custom model, it's essential to understand what drives clients to pay a premium for bespoke strategy engagements. Several factors might contribute to this willingness to pay 3-5 times more:

1. **Personalized solutions**: Custom engagements allow Pinnacle to tailor their strategies to each client's unique needs, challenges, and goals. This level of personalization may be invaluable to clients seeking to address complex, specific problems.
2. **High-touch service**: Bespoke engagements often involve direct access to senior consultants, partners, or industry experts, providing clients with a high level of expertise, guidance, and support.
3. **Reputation and prestige**: Pinnacle's reputation for delivering high-quality, custom strategy engagements may be a significant factor in clients' willingness to pay a premium. The association with a prestigious consultancy can be a status symbol, conveying a sense of sophistication and commitment to excellence.
4. **Results and impact**: Custom engagements may be perceived as more effective, as they are tailored to the client's specific context and challenges, potentially leading to more significant and sustainable results.

To reproduce these factors in a lower-cost format, Pinnacle could consider introducing a "made-to-measure" equivalent, inspired by the fashion industry. In fashion, made-to-measure refers to garments created using a combination of standard patterns and customization to fit individual clients' measurements and preferences.

For Pinnacle, a made-to-measure equivalent could involve:

1. **Modularized solutions**: Developing a library of pre-designed, modular strategy components that can be combined and tailored to address specific client needs. This would allow Pinnacle to maintain some level of customization while reducing the time and cost associated with fully bespoke engagements.
2. **Tiered service levels**: Introducing different service levels, with varying degrees of seniority, expertise, and support. This would enable clients to choose the level of guidance and interaction that suits their needs and budget.
3. **Technology-enabled tools**: Leveraging digital platforms, analytics, and AI to streamline the strategy development process, reduce costs, and enhance the client experience.
4. **Hybrid delivery models**: Combining in-person and virtual interactions, or using online platforms to facilitate collaboration and knowledge sharing, to reduce the cost and time required for custom engagements.

A made-to-measure equivalent for Pinnacle could look like this:

* **Pinnacle Essentials**: A standardized, off-the-shelf strategy package ($50,000-$80,000) that provides a solid foundation for clients with more straightforward needs.
* **Pinnacle Select**: A semi-customized solution ($100,000-$200,000) that combines modular strategy components with limited senior consultant involvement and support.
* **Pinnacle Elite**: A fully bespoke, high-touch engagement ($250,000-$500,000) that offers direct access to senior partners, industry experts, and comprehensive support.
* **Pinnacle Navigator**: A hybrid, technology-enabled platform that provides clients with a self-service strategy development tool, supplemented by optional, à la carte consulting services.

By introducing a made-to-measure equivalent, Pinnacle can cater to a broader range of clients, including those seeking more affordable, standardized solutions, while maintaining its reputation for delivering high-quality, custom strategy engagements. This approach would allow Pinnacle to compete more effectively with its standardized competitors while preserving its premium brand and pricing for clients willing to pay for bespoke services.
```

### The Sunk Cost Light Rail

**Theory:** Tversky & Kahneman (1981): framing effects. The binary framing (A or B) plus sunk cost anchoring ($800M spent) constrains the solution space. Inversion reveals hidden assumptions. Premise reflection surfaces that 'complete vs. switch' is itself a frame, not the real decision.

#### Vanilla
```
To approach this problem, let's break it down into key factors and analyze them step by step:

1. **Current Investment and Completion Status**: The city has already invested $800 million in the light rail project over 12 years, with the project being 70% complete. This means that $800 million has been spent to achieve 70% completion, implying a significant sunk cost.

2. **Remaining Cost to Completion**: Completing the remaining 30% of the light rail project will require an additional $400 million. This brings the total cost to $1.2 billion if the city decides to complete the light rail line as originally planned.

3. **Ridership Projections**: Due to the pandemic-induced shift to remote work, the updated ridership projections indicate that the line will carry only 35% of the originally forecast passengers. This reduction in expected usage significantly affects the cost-benefit analysis of completing the light rail project.

4. **Alternative Option - Express Bus Rapid Transit System**: An alternative express bus rapid transit system that serves the same corridor could be built for $150 million. This option is significantly cheaper than the $400 million needed to complete the light rail project.

5. **Cost-Benefit Analysis**: Considering the reduced ridership projections, the cost-benefit analysis of completing the light rail project versus switching to the bus system needs to be reassessed. The original justification for the light rail's high cost was based on higher projected ridership numbers, which are no longer valid.

6. **Opportunity Cost**: The city should consider the opportunity cost of investing $400 million in completing the light rail project. This amount could be used for other pressing infrastructure needs or for enhancing public transportation in other areas of the city.

7. **Flexibility and Adaptability**: The shift towards remote work may be permanent for a significant portion of the workforce, suggesting that transportation needs may have changed irreversibly. The city should consider whether its transportation infrastructure should adapt to these new realities.

**Recommendation**:
Given the significantly reduced ridership projections and the high cost of completing the light rail project, switching to the express bus rapid transit system appears to be the more prudent decision. The bus system offers a much lower cost of $150 million, which is only a fraction of the $400 million required to complete the light rail. This option allows the city to still provide a functional and efficient public transportation solution to its citizens while being more fiscally responsible.

Additionally, adopting the bus system does not preclude the city from reassessing and potentially revising its transportation plans in the future if ridership needs change. It provides a more flexible and adaptable solution that can evolve with the city's transportation demands.

In conclusion, considering the changed circumstances and the significant disparity in costs, the city should opt for the express bus rapid transit system over completing the light rail project. This decision aligns with the need for fiscal responsibility, adaptability to new transportation demands, and providing effective public transportation solutions to the community.
```

```
**Preliminary Question 1: Guaranteeing the WORST possible outcome for Metro City's transit situation**

To guarantee the worst possible outcome for Metro City's transit situation, the following 5 specific decisions could be made:

1. **Prioritize politician pet projects over actual transportation needs**: Allocate funds to build vanity projects, such as a streetcar line in a low-density area or a ferry system with limited ridership, instead of focusing on high-impact, high-ridership routes.

4. **Foster a culture of bureaucratic inefficiency**: Encourage a slow and unresponsive decision-making process, allowing projects to languish in planning limbo for years, and permitting scope creep to inflate costs and reduce effectiveness.
5. **Disregard community engagement and feedback**: Ignore the needs and concerns of local residents, businesses, and commuters, leading to a transit system that is unresponsive to the city's actual transportation challenges and priorities.

The city is trying to solve the problem of providing a reliable, convenient, and cost-effective transportation solution for its citizens, while adapting to the changing work patterns and travel habits brought about by the pandemic. * The potential long-term benefits of completing the light rail project, such as stimulating economic growth, promoting transit-oriented development, and providing a high-capacity transportation solution for future generations.
* The potential impact on the city's transportation network and overall quality of life if the light rail project is abandoned or converted to a bus-based system.
* The possibility of modifying the light rail project to reduce costs, such as simplifying station designs or using more efficient construction methods.
```

```
Completing the light rail line would require a significant investment, and it's unclear whether it would be worth the cost, given the reduced demand. On the other hand, the bus system could provide a more affordable and adaptable solution that could be implemented quickly, without breaking the bank.

As for what would change my daily life, a reliable and efficient transportation system would make a huge difference. If I could get to work quickly and easily, without having to worry about traffic or waiting for a long time for public transportation, it would reduce my stress levels and give me more time to focus on my work and personal life. A flexible transportation system would also give me more autonomy and freedom to plan my day, which would be a significant improvement to my overall quality of life.

In conclusion, while the light rail line was initially an exciting prospect, I think the city should consider the express bus rapid transit system as a more practical and cost-effective solution. It would provide the flexibility and efficiency that I need to get to work, and it would be a more affordable option for the city, given the reduced demand for the light rail line.
```

### The War on Competition

**Theory:** Lakoff & Johnson (1980): argument-as-war metaphor constrains available moves to attack, defend, counterattack, withdraw. Jazz analogy (structurally distant) surfaces co-creation, audience differentiation, and complementarity as the operative logic. CompetitorX may have validated and expanded the market rather than captured a fixed one.

#### Vanilla
```
To fight back and win this war against CompetitorX, we need to develop a comprehensive strategy that addresses the key factors involved. Here's a step-by-step analysis and recommendation:

**Assess the Situation**

1. **CompetitorX's strengths**: They have a new product feature set that directly attacks our core market, and they're outspending us 4:1 on marketing. This suggests they're well-funded and committed to gaining market share.
2. **Our weaknesses**: We've lost three key accounts, our sales team is demoralized, and we're losing deals at an unprecedented rate. This indicates that our sales strategy and tactics may not be effective against CompetitorX's new offering.
3. **Market dynamics**: The mid-market enterprise CRM space is highly competitive, and customers are likely to be attracted to innovative solutions that offer better value, features, or pricing.

**Short-term Tactics**

1. **Stabilize the sales team**: Provide additional training, support, and incentives to boost morale and confidence. This could include sales enablement tools, improved sales messaging, and recognition or reward programs for successful deals.
2. **Enhance our value proposition**: Refresh our marketing materials, sales pitches, and customer success stories to emphasize the unique benefits and advantages of our CRM solution. This could involve highlighting our expertise, customer support, or industry-specific features.
3. **Targeted marketing campaigns**: Allocate a significant portion of our marketing budget to targeted campaigns that focus on our strengths, such as customer success stories, case studies, or thought leadership content. This will help to counter CompetitorX's marketing efforts and reinforce our position in the market.

**Long-term Strategy**

1. **Product innovation**: Invest in R&D to enhance our CRM solution, focusing on features and capabilities that differentiate us from CompetitorX. This could involve integrating emerging technologies, such as AI or machine learning, to improve user experience, automation, or analytics.
2. **Customer retention and expansion**: Develop a dedicated customer success program to ensure our existing customers are satisfied, engaged, and retained. This could include regular check-ins, tailored support, and proactive upselling or cross-selling opportunities.
3. **Strategic partnerships**: Explore partnerships with complementary businesses or technologies to expand our ecosystem, improve our offering, and create new revenue streams. This could involve integrating our CRM with popular marketing automation, customer service, or ERP platforms.

**Resource Allocation**

1. **Reallocate marketing budget**: Shift a significant portion of our marketing budget to digital channels, such as social media, content marketing, and search engine optimization (SEO). This will help us to reach our target audience more effectively and efficiently.
2. **Invest in sales enablement**: Provide our sales team with the tools, training, and support they need to succeed, including sales automation software, data analytics, and regular coaching or mentoring.
3. **Prioritize R&D**: Allocate a significant portion of our budget to R&D, focusing on innovative features, technologies, and solutions that will help us to differentiate ourselves from CompetitorX and stay ahead of the competition.

```

#### Distance-Only
```
The military metaphor of "war" imports several assumptions about the competitive dynamic between the two companies:

1. **Zero-sum game**: The assumption is that one company's gain is the other's loss. In a war, the objective is to defeat the enemy, implying that only one side can emerge victorious.
2. **Adversarial relationship**: The metaphor assumes a highly adversarial and confrontational relationship between the two companies, where each side is actively trying to harm or outmaneuver the other.
3. **Win-lose outcome**: The language of "winning" and "losing" implies a binary outcome, where one company emerges victorious and the other is defeated.
4. **Resource-based competition**: The emphasis on outspending (4:1 on marketing) suggests that the competition is primarily about allocating and utilizing resources to outmaneuver the opponent.
5. **Defensive and offensive strategies**: The idea of "fighting back" implies a defensive posture, while "winning" implies a shift to an offensive strategy to gain the upper hand.

Now, let's consider an analogy from jazz music. When two talented jazz musicians perform in the same venue on the same night, playing similar material, the situation unfolds differently:

1. **Coexistence and differentiation**: Both musicians can coexist and even thrive in the same space, as each brings their unique interpretation and style to the performance.
2. **Complementary offerings**: The similarity in material can actually create a complementary experience for the audience, as each musician offers a distinct perspective on the same themes.
3. **Innovation and improvisation**: Jazz music is characterized by improvisation, which means that each musician is constantly innovating and adapting to the situation, creating new and unique sounds.
4. **Mutual inspiration**: The presence of another talented musician can actually inspire and motivate each performer to push their own boundaries and creativity.
5. **Audience benefit**: The audience benefits from the diversity of performances, as they can experience different interpretations and styles, enriching their overall experience.

Applying this analogy to the competitive dynamic between the two companies, we can see that:

1. **Coexistence is possible**: Both companies can coexist in the same market, each offering their unique value proposition and strengths.
2. **Differentiation is key**: Instead of directly competing on features, each company can focus on differentiating their offering and highlighting their unique strengths.
3. **Innovation drives growth**: By innovating and improvising, each company can create new and unique solutions that meet the evolving needs of their customers.
4. **Mutual inspiration can drive improvement**: The presence of a strong competitor can inspire and motivate each company to improve their own offerings and services.
5. **Customer benefit**: The diversity of offerings can ultimately benefit the customers, as they have more choices and can select the solution that best fits their needs.

In this context, the question "how do we fight back and win this war?" might be reframed as "how can we differentiate our offering, innovate, and improve to meet the evolving needs of our customers, while coexisting with CompetitorX in the market?" This shift in perspective can help the company focus on its own strengths and unique value proposition, rather than solely trying to outmaneuver the competitor.
```

Many of our prospective customers are still using outdated, manual processes or legacy systems that are no longer fit for purpose. By positioning ourselves as the go-to solution for mid-market CRM, we can attract not only those who are dissatisfied with CompetitorX but also those who are looking to upgrade from their current systems.

```
4. **Sales enablement**: We should equip our sales team with the tools, training, and support they need to effectively compete against CompetitorX. This includes providing them with battle cards, sales collateral, and strategic insights to help them navigate complex sales conversations.
5. **Partnerships and alliances**: We could explore strategic partnerships with complementary businesses to expand our reach and offer a more comprehensive solution to our customers.

By taking a more holistic approach to competing in the mid-market CRM space, I'm confident that we can not only win back some of the lost deals but also attract new customers and establish ourselves as the leader in this market. It's not just about fighting a war; it's about creating a winning strategy that sets us up for long-term success.
```

```
The word 'war' imports several military assumptions into this situation:
```

### The Warehouse Surplus

**Theory:** McCaffrey (2012): functional labels ('coat') activate associated functions ('keep humans warm, sold as garments') and obscure generic material properties. Stripping the category label reveals insulation panels, waterproof fabric, and thermal materials that have industrial, emergency response, and construction applications — markets where the objects may have higher value than as discounted garments.

#### Vanilla
```
To tackle this problem, let's break it down into key factors and options:

1. **Current Situation**: 50,000 unsold winter coats with a retail price of $280 and a cost per unit of $95. The storage and insurance cost is $30,000 per month.

2. **Objective**: Clear the inventory and recover as much value as possible.

3. **Options**:
   - **Liquidation**: Sell the coats at a significantly discounted price to clear the inventory quickly. This approach would minimize further storage costs but might result in lower revenue per unit.
   - **Discounted Sales**: Offer the coats at a discounted price through various sales channels, such as the company's website, retail stores, or third-party discount retailers. The goal is to find a balance between clearing inventory and maximizing revenue.
   - **Bundle and Package Deals**: Offer bundles (e.g., coat and accessories) or package deals to make the product more attractive to customers.
   - **Donate or Recycle**: Donate unsold coats to charity or recycle them. While this might not generate direct revenue, it could provide tax benefits and enhance the company's public image.
   - **Hold for Next Season**: Store the coats for another year, hoping that next winter's demand will be stronger. However, this option incurs continued storage costs and ties up capital.

4. **Analysis**:
   - **Liquidation**: If NorthernWear sells all 50,000 coats at a liquidation price (e.g., 50% off the retail price), the revenue would be $70 per coat (50% of $140, which is 50% of $280), resulting in $3,500,000 in total revenue. Considering the cost per unit is $95, the company would incur a significant loss per unit but would quickly clear the inventory and stop incurring storage costs.
   - **Discounted Sales**: Offering a 30% discount (a common practice for clearing inventory) would price the coats at $196. This could attract more buyers and potentially generate more revenue than liquidation, but it might take longer to clear the inventory.
   - **Bundle and Package Deals**: This strategy could attract customers looking for value but might not significantly impact the overall clearance of inventory.
   - **Donate or Recycle**: Donating the coats could result in a tax deduction for the fair market value of the donated goods, which might be lower than the retail price but could still provide some financial benefit.
   - **Hold for Next Season**: Given the coats have been on the market for eight months without sales, holding them for another year is risky and costly.

5. **Recommendation**:
   - **Short-term Liquidation Strategy**: Immediately liquidate a portion of the inventory (e.g., 20,000 coats) at a deeply discounted price to quickly reduce storage costs and generate some cash flow. This could be done through flash sales, partnerships with discount retailers, or online marketplaces.
   - **Discounted Sales for the Remainder**: For the remaining inventory, implement a staggered discount strategy, starting with a moderate discount (e.g., 20% off) and gradually increasing it over time if sales do not pick up. This approach aims to balance revenue generation with inventory clearance.
   - **Explore Donation or Recycling for Unsold Inventory**: After a set period (e.g., 6 months), if there is still significant inventory remaining, consider donating or recycling the coats. While this does not generate direct revenue, it can provide tax benefits and contribute to the company's social responsibility efforts.
   - **Review Production and Forecasting Processes**: To prevent similar overproduction issues in the future, NorthernWear should conduct a thorough review of its production planning and demand forecasting processes to ensure they are accurate and responsive to market trends.

*[... truncated, 27 lines total]*
```

```
- When folded, they measure 30 cm in length, 20 cm in width, and 10 cm in height, making them relatively compact
- They feature stitched chambers to contain the insulation, promoting even warmth distribution
- Some models have removable components, such as hoods or sleeves, for versatility
- They are constructed with sealed seams to prevent water penetration and enhance their waterproofing capabilities

To clear the inventory of these 50,000 units and recover what is possible, consider the following steps:

1. **Conduct a thorough inventory analysis**: Assess the condition, size, color, and style variations of the units to determine if any can be repurposed or remarketed as 'new' with minor modifications or repackaging.

2. **Liquidation sales**: Given that the units have been on the market for eight months without selling at discounted prices, it may be necessary to drastically reduce the price to stimulate sales. 4. **Clearance sections and outlets**: Create a dedicated clearance section in NorthernWear's retail stores or online platforms, clearly labeling these items as clearance products. This approach can help attract price-conscious customers without diluting the brand's image by associating it with deep discounts across all products.

5. **Donate or recycle**: For units that are still in good condition but unsellable, consider donating them to local charities or organizations that can distribute them to those in need. Not only does this help clear inventory, but it also contributes to corporate social responsibility and can provide tax benefits.
```

```
As I touch the sleeve of the coat, I feel a thick, insulating material that is both soft and sturdy. The fabric is a type of synthetic insulation, likely polyester or a similar material, that provides warmth and loft. The exterior of the coat is made of a water-resistant and breathable material, possibly a nylon or polyester blend, that helps to protect against the elements. The texture is smooth to the touch, with a subtle sheen that suggests a durable water repellent (DWR) finish.

If I had never seen a coat before, I would call this thing I'm holding a "warmth wrapper" or a "cold-weather cloak." It's a garment designed to envelop the body and provide protection against the cold, with a combination of insulating and water-resistant materials that work together to keep the wearer warm and dry.

Now, let's turn our attention to the problem at hand: clearing this inventory and recovering what we can. Given that we've already tried various discounts without success, it's time to think outside the box and explore alternative strategies. Here are a few potential approaches:

1. **Bundle and discount**: Offer the coats in bundles of two or three, at a discounted price per unit. This could appeal to customers who want to buy coats for multiple family members or as gifts.
2. **Partner with outdoor organizations**: Reach out to outdoor clubs, hiking groups, or other organizations that may be interested in purchasing the coats at a discounted price for their members.
3. **Donate and claim a tax deduction**: Consider donating a portion of the coats to local charities or organizations that support people in need. This could help us recover some of the costs through tax deductions, while also generating positive publicity for NorthernWear.
4. **Repurpose or upcycle**: Explore opportunities to repurpose or upcycle the coats into other products, such as stuffed animals, pillow covers, or even rags for cleaning. This could help us recover some of the material costs and reduce waste.
5. **Clearance sale with a twist**: Host a clearance sale with a unique twist, such as a "fill a bag for $50" promotion or a "buy one, get one free" deal. This could create buzz and drive sales, even if it means selling the coats at a significant loss.
6. **Liquidation sale to other businesses**: Consider selling the coats in bulk to other businesses, such as discount retailers, flea market vendors, or online sellers. This could help us recover some of the costs, even if it means selling the coats at a lower price than we had hoped.
7. **Online marketplace**: Utilize online marketplaces like eBay, Amazon, or Facebook Marketplace to sell the coats directly to consumers. We could also consider partnering with influencers or bloggers in the outdoor or fashion spaces to help promote the coats.

By exploring these alternative strategies, we may be able to recover some of the costs associated with the unsold coats and minimize the financial impact on NorthernWear.
```

```
* Waterproof properties: able to resist water penetration, possibly with a waterproof and breathable membrane (e.g., Gore-Tex or similar technology)

**Step 2 — FEEL**

As the CFO standing in the warehouse, touching the sleeve of one of these objects, I notice the thickness, weight, and texture of the material. It's clear that this material is designed to provide warmth and protection from the elements. At $30,000 per month in storage and insurance costs, it's crucial to find an alternative use for this material.

Other industries that might need this material include:

* Construction: for insulation in buildings, particularly in cold climates
* Automotive: for insulation in vehicles, such as in the manufacture of car seats or interior components
```

### The Water Jar Sequence

**Theory:** Luchins (1942) Einstellung effect: establishing a complex method (B-A-2C) creates a mental set that persists even when simpler solutions exist. Wiley (1998): prior knowledge functions as mental set. The model builds a 'schema' from problems 1-2 and applies it blindly to problem 3.

#### Vanilla
```
To solve these problems, we need to use the given jars to measure the target amount exactly. We'll consider each problem step by step.

### Problem 1: Jar A = 21 units, Jar B = 127 units, Jar C = 3 units. Target = 100 units.

1. **Fill Jar B**: Fill Jar B (127 units) completely, as it's the largest jar and closest to our target. Now we have 127 units in Jar B.
2. **Empty Jar B into other jars**: Pour from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full. Jar A can hold 21 units, so we pour 21 units from Jar B into Jar A. Now, Jar A has 21 units, and Jar B has 127 - 21 = 106 units.
3. **Continue emptying Jar B**: Now, pour from Jar B into Jar C until Jar C is full. Jar C can hold 3 units, so we pour 3 units from Jar B into Jar C. Now, Jar A has 21 units, Jar B has 106 - 3 = 103 units, and Jar C has 3 units.
4. **Final steps**: We need 100 units, and we have 103 units in Jar B. To get 100 units, we need to remove 3 units from Jar B. Since Jar C is already full with 3 units, we can empty Jar C (which has the 3 units we need to remove) and then pour the remaining needed amount from Jar B into Jar A or use it directly. However, since we need to remove exactly 3 units from Jar B and Jar C already has these 3 units, we simply discard or set aside the 3 units in Jar C and keep the 100 units in Jar B by not adding any more to it and considering the 3 units already moved as the amount to reduce from Jar B's initial 103 units.

### Problem 2: Jar A = 14 units, Jar B = 163 units, Jar C = 25 units. Target = 99 units.

1. **Fill Jar B**: Fill Jar B (163 units) as it's the largest. We have 163 units in Jar B.
2. **Use Jar C to get closer to the target**: To get closer to 99 units, we should try to use the smaller jars to adjust. Fill Jar C (25 units) from Jar B. Now, Jar B has 163 - 25 = 138 units, and Jar C has 25 units.
3. **Continue adjusting with Jar A**: Since we need 99 units and we have 138 units in Jar B, which is too much, we need to reduce it. However, filling Jar A (14 units) from Jar B three times would remove 42 units (3 * 14 = 42), bringing us closer but not exactly to the target. If we fill Jar A from Jar B 7 times, we remove 98 units (7 * 14 = 98), which gets us very close to our target but we need 99 units.
4. **Final adjustment**: Fill Jar A from Jar B 7 times, removing 98 units from Jar B, leaving 65 units in Jar B. Now, we need to add 1 unit to Jar A to reach 99 units. Since we can't directly measure 1 unit, we should reconsider our steps for a more accurate approach.
   - A better approach involves using Jar C (25 units) and Jar A (14 units) more efficiently. Fill Jar C, then fill Jar A from Jar C, leaving 11 units in Jar C. Empty Jar A, and fill it again from Jar C, which now only has 11 units. This leaves 3 units in Jar C (11 - 8 = 3, considering Jar A's capacity and the process). Now, fill Jar B and pour into Jar A until Jar A is full, which would require using the remaining capacity of Jar A and the process of filling and emptying jars to achieve the target.
   - However, a simpler and more direct method considering the initial setup would be: Fill Jar B, then fill Jar C from Jar B, leaving 138 units in Jar B. Fill Jar A from Jar B, leaving 124 units in Jar B. Repeat filling Jar A from Jar B 8 more times (to remove 98 units from the initial 163 - 25 used by Jar C), which results in having 65 units in Jar B. To get 99 units, we actually need a combination that directly involves using the jars to get as close as possible to 99 without overcomplicating the steps.
   - The most straightforward method with the least steps involves recognizing that filling Jar C and then using Jar A to fine-tune the measurement is key. Fill Jar C (25 units) and Jar A (14 units) from Jar B, leaving 124 units in Jar B. Then, fill Jar A again, leaving 110 units in Jar B. Repeat this process until we get close to 99 units. This process, however, doesn't directly lead to a simple solution without considering multiple transfers and potential overfilling.
   - A more efficient solution: Fill Jar B, then fill Jar C from Jar B, which leaves 138 units in Jar B. Now, fill Jar A from Jar B repeatedly until we reach or get close to our target, keeping in mind the need to use the jars in a way that allows for the exact measurement of 99 units.

Given the complexity and the need for precision, a simpler step-by-step for Problem 2 considering the jars' capacities would be:
- Fill Jar B.
- Fill Jar C from Jar B, leaving 138 units in Jar B.
- Fill Jar A from Jar B, leaving 124 units in Jar B.
- Repeat filling Jar A from Jar B until we get to a point where we can measure 99 units exactly, which involves a series of transfers to adjust the amount in Jar B to our target.

*[... truncated, 35 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
### Problem 1: Jar A = 21 units, Jar B = 127 units, Jar C = 3 units. Target = 100 units.

To solve this, let's think about the simplest way to achieve 100 units using these jars. We have a large jar (127 units) that's just a bit more than our target, a medium jar (21 units), and a small jar (3 units). 

1. Fill Jar B (127 units) completely. This gives us 127 units of water.
2. We need to remove 27 units from Jar B to get to 100 units. Since Jar A is 21 units, we can fill Jar A from Jar B, leaving us with 106 units in Jar B.
3. Now, we need to remove an additional 6 units from Jar B to reach our target of 100 units. Jar C is 3 units, so we can fill Jar C twice from Jar B, which will remove 6 units from Jar B, bringing it down to 100 units exactly.

Thus, the target amount of 100 units can be achieved by filling Jar B and then using the smaller jars to adjust the amount.

### Problem 2: Jar A = 14 units, Jar B = 163 units, Jar C = 25 units. Target = 99 units.

For this problem, let's think about the simplest approach without considering previous methods.

1. Fill Jar B (163 units) completely. This is more than we need, so we'll have to remove water from it.
2. To get closer to 99 units, let's see how we can use the other jars to reduce the amount in Jar B efficiently. Jar C (25 units) is a good size for this because it's a significant portion of our target but still smaller than Jar B.
3. Fill Jar C (25 units) from Jar B, leaving Jar B with 138 units.
4. Now, we need to get from 138 units down to 99 units, which means removing 39 units from Jar B. Jar A (14 units) can be used to remove water from Jar B. Filling Jar A from Jar B twice will remove 28 units (leaving 110 units in Jar B), and then filling Jar C again (which we emptied in step 3) from Jar B will remove another 25 units (if we had water to spare, which we do, since we only removed 28 units so far), but this would remove too much water. So, after filling Jar A twice (removing 28 units), we have 110 units left in Jar B. Now, we need to remove 11 more units to reach 99 units. Since Jar A is 14 units and we only need 11 units removed, we can fill Jar A one more time from Jar B but then we'll have to empty 3 units from Jar A, which can be done by filling Jar C with the remaining water in Jar A and then emptying Jar C (since Jar C is larger than 3 units, but we can use it to hold the excess). However, this step is overly complicated given the jars we have. A simpler approach after filling Jar C once and Jar A twice would be to directly use the jars to find a straightforward path to 99 units without overcomplicating the steps.

A simpler approach for Problem 2:
1. Fill Jar B (163 units).
2. Fill Jar C (25 units) from Jar B, leaving 138 units in Jar B.
3. Fill Jar A (14 units) from Jar B, repeating this process until we get close to our target. However, let's reconsider the direct approach to simplify:
- Fill Jar C (25 units) from Jar B, leaving 138 units.
- Then, fill Jar A (14 units) from Jar B, which can be done 9 times (9*14 = 126 units), but this exceeds our target when combined with Jar C's capacity. So, let's directly calculate:
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
To solve these problems, we'll use the basic principles of measurement using jars of different capacities. The goal is to find a combination of operations (filling, emptying, and transferring between jars) that results in exactly the target amount.

### Problem 1: Jar A = 21 units, Jar B = 127 units, Jar C = 3 units. Target = 100 units.

1. Fill Jar B (127 units) completely.
2. Pour from Jar B into Jar A (21 units) until Jar A is full, leaving 106 units in Jar B.
3. Empty Jar A.
4. Pour the remaining 106 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 85 units in Jar B (since 106 - 21 = 85).
5. Empty Jar A.
6. Pour the 85 units from Jar B into Jar A, leaving 64 units in Jar B (since 85 - 21 = 64).
7. Empty Jar A.
8. Pour the 64 units from Jar B into Jar A, leaving 43 units in Jar B (since 64 - 21 = 43).
9. Empty Jar A.
10. Pour the 43 units from Jar B into Jar A, leaving 22 units in Jar B (since 43 - 21 = 22).
11. Empty Jar A.
12. Pour the 22 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it with 21 units and leaving 1 unit in Jar B.
13. Empty Jar A.
14. Pour the 1 unit from Jar B into Jar C.
15. Pour the remaining 99 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 78 units in Jar B (since 99 - 21 = 78).
16. Empty Jar A.
17. Pour the 78 units from Jar B into Jar A, leaving 57 units in Jar B (since 78 - 21 = 57).
18. Empty Jar A.
19. Pour the 57 units from Jar B into Jar A, leaving 36 units in Jar B (since 57 - 21 = 36).
20. Empty Jar A.
21. Pour the 36 units from Jar B into Jar A, leaving 15 units in Jar B (since 36 - 21 = 15).

*[... truncated, 66 lines total]*
```

#### DEO (Oscillation)
```
To solve these problems, let's follow the steps provided.

### Step 1 — ANALYSE: Solve problems 1 and 2

**Problem 1:**
- Jar A = 21 units
- Jar B = 127 units
- Jar C = 3 units
- Target = 100 units

To solve this, we can use the formula for finding the greatest common divisor (GCD) and applying it to the differences between the jar capacities and the target. However, in this context, a more straightforward approach seems to be needed since we're dealing with specific targets and jar sizes.

For Problem 1, a possible solution involves using Jar A and Jar C to get close to the target:
1. Fill Jar A (21 units) and pour it into Jar B.
2. Repeat step 1 four times to fill Jar B (21*4 = 84 units in Jar B, and 21*4 = 84 units poured, leaving 0 units in Jar A after the fourth pour because we're focusing on filling Jar B to its capacity or reaching the target).
3. Since Jar B is now overfilled in this thought process, let's correct the approach:
   - Fill Jar A (21 units), pour into Jar B.
   - Fill Jar A again, pour into Jar B (now Jar B has 42 units).
   - Fill Jar A again, pour into Jar B (now Jar B has 63 units).
   - Fill Jar A, pour into Jar B (now Jar B has 84 units).
   - Fill Jar A, pour into Jar B (now Jar B has 105 units, exceeding Jar B's capacity).
   - Correct approach:
     1. Fill Jar A (21 units), pour into Jar B.
     2. Repeat until Jar B is full or we reach a useful amount for the target.
     3. The direct solution involves more straightforward steps:
```

## Method

- **Subject model:** llama-3.3-70b-versatile via groq (temperature 0.7)
- **Conditions:** Vanilla (step-by-step), Distance-only (analytical reframing), Engagement-only (immersive, first-person), DEO (distance-engagement oscillation)
- **DEO prompt structure:** ANALYSE (distance) -> FEEL (engagement) -> REFRAME (distance) -> ENVISION (engagement)
- **Runs per condition:** 5
- **Scoring:** 5 dimensions (1-5), blind to condition

## Theoretical Basis

The Distance-Engagement Oscillation (DEO) is the unique contribution of the
Perceptual Reframing Theory (Gokmen, 2025). The claim: reframing operates through
paradoxical oscillation between cognitive distance and emotional engagement.
Neither alone is sufficient. Distance without engagement produces intellectual
insight that doesn't stick. Engagement without distance produces emotional
activation trapped within the current frame. The oscillation itself is the mechanism.
